BEG_SENT “But,” he added, “you will of course wish to have your humble respects delivered to them, with your grateful thanks for their kindness to you while you have been here.” Elizabeth made no objection; the door was then allowed to be shut, and the carriage drove off.
BEG_SENT “Good gracious!” cried Maria, after a few minutes’ silence, “it seems but a day or two since we first came!
BEG_SENT and yet how many things have happened!” “A great many indeed,” said her companion with a sigh.
BEG_SENT “We have dined nine times at Rosings, besides drinking tea there twice!
BEG_SENT How much I shall have to tell!” Elizabeth added privately, “And how much I shall have to conceal!” Their journey was performed without much conversation, or any alarm; and within four hours of their leaving Hunsford they reached Mr. Gardiner’s house, where they were to remain a few days.
BEG_SENT Jane looked well, and Elizabeth had little opportunity of studying her spirits, amidst the various engagements which the kindness of her aunt had reserved for them.
BEG_SENT But Jane was to go home with her, and at Longbourn there would be leisure enough for observation.
BEG_SENT It was not without an effort, meanwhile, that she could wait even for Longbourn, before she told her sister of Mr. Darcy’s proposals.
BEG_SENT To know that she had the power of revealing what would so exceedingly astonish Jane, and must, at the same time, so highly gratify whatever of her own vanity she had not yet been able to reason away, was such a temptation to openness as nothing could have conquered but the state of indecision in which she remained as to the extent of what she should communicate; and her fear, if she once entered on the subject, of being hurried into repeating something of Bingley which might only grieve her sister further.
BEG_SENT   It was the second week in May, in which the three young ladies set out together from Gracechurch Street for the town of ——, in Hertfordshire; and, as they drew near the appointed inn where Mr. Bennet’s carriage was to meet them, they quickly perceived, in token of the coachman’s punctuality, both Kitty and Lydia looking out of a dining-room up stairs.
BEG_SENT These two girls had been above an hour in the place, happily employed in visiting an opposite milliner, watching the sentinel on guard, and dressing a salad and cucumber.
BEG_SENT After welcoming their sisters, they triumphantly displayed a table set out with such cold meat as an inn larder usually affords, exclaiming, “Is not this nice?
BEG_SENT Is not this an agreeable surprise?” “And we mean to treat you all,” added Lydia, “but you must lend us the money, for we have just spent ours at the shop out there.” Then, showing her purchases—“Look here, I have bought this bonnet.
BEG_SENT I do not think it is very pretty; but I thought I might as well buy it as not.
BEG_SENT I shall pull it to pieces as soon as I get home, and see if I can make it up any better.” And when her sisters abused it as ugly, she added, with perfect unconcern, “Oh!
BEG_SENT but there were two or three much uglier in the shop; and when I have bought some prettier-coloured satin to trim it with fresh, I think it will be very tolerable.
BEG_SENT Besides, it will not much signify what one wears this summer, after the ——shire have left Meryton, and they are going in a fortnight.” “Are they indeed!” cried Elizabeth, with the greatest satisfaction.
BEG_SENT “They are going to be encamped near Brighton; and I do so want papa to take us all there for the summer!
BEG_SENT It would be such a delicious scheme; and I dare say would hardly cost anything at all.
BEG_SENT Mamma would like to go too of all things!
BEG_SENT Only think what a miserable summer else we shall have!” “Yes,” thought Elizabeth, “_that_ would be a delightful scheme indeed, and completely do for us at once.
BEG_SENT Good Heaven!
BEG_SENT Brighton, and a whole campful of soldiers, to us, who have been overset already by one poor regiment of militia, and the monthly balls of Meryton!” “Now I have got some news for you,” said Lydia, as they sat down at table.
BEG_SENT “What do you think?
BEG_SENT It is excellent news—capital news—and about a certain person we all like!” Jane and Elizabeth looked at each other, and the waiter was told he need not stay.
BEG_SENT Lydia laughed, and said: “Aye, that is just like your formality and discretion.
BEG_SENT You thought the waiter must not hear, as if he cared!
BEG_SENT I dare say he often hears worse things said than I am going to say.
BEG_SENT But he is an ugly fellow!
BEG_SENT I am glad he is gone.
BEG_SENT I never saw such a long chin in my life.
BEG_SENT Well, but now for my news; it is about dear Wickham; too good for the waiter, is it not?
BEG_SENT There is no danger of Wickham’s marrying Mary King.
BEG_SENT There’s for you!
BEG_SENT She is gone down to her uncle at Liverpool: gone to stay.
BEG_SENT Wickham is safe.” “And Mary King is safe!” added Elizabeth; “safe from a connection imprudent as to fortune.” “She is a great fool for going away, if she liked him.” “But I hope there is no strong attachment on either side,” said Jane.
BEG_SENT “I am sure there is not on _his_.
BEG_SENT I will answer for it, he never cared three straws about her—who _could_ about such a nasty little freckled thing?” Elizabeth was shocked to think that, however incapable of such coarseness of _expression_ herself, the coarseness of the _sentiment_ was little other than her own breast had harboured and fancied liberal!
BEG_SENT As soon as all had ate, and the elder ones paid, the carriage was ordered; and after some contrivance, the whole party, with all their boxes, work-bags, and parcels, and the unwelcome addition of Kitty’s and Lydia’s purchases, were seated in it.
BEG_SENT “How nicely we are all crammed in,” cried Lydia.
BEG_SENT “I am glad I bought my bonnet, if it is only for the fun of having another bandbox!
BEG_SENT Well, now let us be quite comfortable and snug, and talk and laugh all the way home.
BEG_SENT And in the first place, let us hear what has happened to you all since you went away.
BEG_SENT Have you seen any pleasant men?
BEG_SENT Have you had any flirting?
BEG_SENT I was in great hopes that one of you would have got a husband before you came back.
BEG_SENT Jane will be quite an old maid soon, I declare.
BEG_SENT She is almost three-and-twenty!
BEG_SENT Lord, how ashamed I should be of not being married before three-and-twenty!
BEG_SENT My aunt Phillips wants you so to get husbands, you can’t think.
BEG_SENT She says Lizzy had better have taken Mr. Collins; but _I_ do not think there would have been any fun in it.
BEG_SENT Lord!
BEG_SENT how I should like to be married before any of you; and then I would _chaperon_ you about to all the balls.
BEG_SENT Dear me!
BEG_SENT we had such a good piece of fun the other day at Colonel Forster’s.
BEG_SENT Kitty and me were to spend the day there, and Mrs. Forster promised to have a little dance in the evening; (by the bye, Mrs. Forster and me are _such_ friends!)
BEG_SENT and so she asked the two Harringtons to come, but Harriet was ill, and so Pen was forced to come by herself; and then, what do you think we did?
BEG_SENT We dressed up Chamberlayne in woman’s clothes on purpose to pass for a lady, only think what fun!
BEG_SENT Not a soul knew of it, but Colonel and Mrs. Forster, and Kitty and me, except my aunt, for we were forced to borrow one of her gowns; and you cannot imagine how well he looked!
BEG_SENT When Denny, and Wickham, and Pratt, and two or three more of the men came in, they did not know him in the least.
BEG_SENT Lord!
BEG_SENT how I laughed!
BEG_SENT and so did Mrs. Forster.
BEG_SENT I thought I should have died.
BEG_SENT And _that_ made the men suspect something, and then they soon found out what was the matter.” With such kinds of histories of their parties and good jokes, did Lydia, assisted by Kitty’s hints and additions, endeavour to amuse her companions all the way to Longbourn.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth listened as little as she could, but there was no escaping the frequent mention of Wickham’s name.
BEG_SENT Their reception at home was most kind.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet rejoiced to see Jane in undiminished beauty; and more than once during dinner did Mr. Bennet say voluntarily to Elizabeth: “I am glad you are come back, Lizzy.” Their party in the dining-room was large, for almost all the Lucases came to meet Maria and hear the news; and various were the subjects that occupied them: Lady Lucas was inquiring of Maria, after the welfare and poultry of her eldest daughter; Mrs. Bennet was doubly engaged, on one hand collecting an account of the present fashions from Jane, who sat some way below her, and, on the other, retailing them all to the younger Lucases; and Lydia, in a voice rather louder than any other person’s, was enumerating the various pleasures of the morning to anybody who would hear her.
BEG_SENT “Oh!
BEG_SENT Mary,” said she, “I wish you had gone with us, for we had such fun!
BEG_SENT As we went along, Kitty and I drew up the blinds, and pretended there was nobody in the coach; and I should have gone so all the way, if Kitty had not been sick; and when we got to the George, I do think we behaved very handsomely, for we treated the other three with the nicest cold luncheon in the world, and if you would have gone, we would have treated you too.
BEG_SENT And then when we came away it was such fun!
BEG_SENT I thought we never should have got into the coach.
BEG_SENT I was ready to die of laughter.
BEG_SENT And then we were so merry all the way home!
BEG_SENT we talked and laughed so loud, that anybody might have heard us ten miles off!” To this Mary very gravely replied, “Far be it from me, my dear sister, to depreciate such pleasures!
BEG_SENT They would doubtless be congenial with the generality of female minds.
BEG_SENT But I confess they would have no charms for _me_—I should infinitely prefer a book.” But of this answer Lydia heard not a word.
BEG_SENT She seldom listened to anybody for more than half a minute, and never attended to Mary at all.
BEG_SENT In the afternoon Lydia was urgent with the rest of the girls to walk to Meryton, and to see how everybody went on; but Elizabeth steadily opposed the scheme.
BEG_SENT It should not be said that the Miss Bennets could not be at home half a day before they were in pursuit of the officers.
BEG_SENT There was another reason too for her opposition.
BEG_SENT She dreaded seeing Mr. Wickham again, and was resolved to avoid it as long as possible.
BEG_SENT The comfort to _her_ of the regiment’s approaching removal was indeed beyond expression.
BEG_SENT In a fortnight they were to go—and once gone, she hoped there could be nothing more to plague her on his account.
BEG_SENT She had not been many hours at home before she found that the Brighton scheme, of which Lydia had given them a hint at the inn, was under frequent discussion between her parents.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth saw directly that her father had not the smallest intention of yielding; but his answers were at the same time so vague and equivocal, that her mother, though often disheartened, had never yet despaired of succeeding at last.
BEG_SENT   Elizabeth’s impatience to acquaint Jane with what had happened could no longer be overcome; and at length, resolving to suppress every particular in which her sister was concerned, and preparing her to be surprised, she related to her the next morning the chief of the scene between Mr. Darcy and herself.
BEG_SENT Miss Bennet’s astonishment was soon lessened by the strong sisterly partiality which made any admiration of Elizabeth appear perfectly natural; and all surprise was shortly lost in other feelings.
BEG_SENT She was sorry that Mr. Darcy should have delivered his sentiments in a manner so little suited to recommend them; but still more was she grieved for the unhappiness which her sister’s refusal must have given him.
BEG_SENT “His being so sure of succeeding was wrong,” said she, “and certainly ought not to have appeared; but consider how much it must increase his disappointment!” “Indeed,” replied Elizabeth, “I am heartily sorry for him; but he has other feelings, which will probably soon drive away his regard for me.
BEG_SENT You do not blame me, however, for refusing him?” “Blame you!
BEG_SENT Oh, no.” “But you blame me for having spoken so warmly of Wickham?” “No—I do not know that you were wrong in saying what you did.” “But you _will_ know it, when I tell you what happened the very next day.” She then spoke of the letter, repeating the whole of its contents as far as they concerned George Wickham.
BEG_SENT What a stroke was this for poor Jane!
BEG_SENT who would willingly have gone through the world without believing that so much wickedness existed in the whole race of mankind, as was here collected in one individual.
BEG_SENT Nor was Darcy’s vindication, though grateful to her feelings, capable of consoling her for such discovery.
BEG_SENT Most earnestly did she labour to prove the probability of error, and seek to clear the one without involving the other.
BEG_SENT “This will not do,” said Elizabeth; “you never will be able to make both of them good for anything.
BEG_SENT Take your choice, but you must be satisfied with only one.
BEG_SENT There is but such a quantity of merit between them; just enough to make one good sort of man; and of late it has been shifting about pretty much.
BEG_SENT For my part, I am inclined to believe it all Darcy’s; but you shall do as you choose.” It was some time, however, before a smile could be extorted from Jane.
BEG_SENT “I do not know when I have been more shocked,” said she.
BEG_SENT “Wickham so very bad!
BEG_SENT It is almost past belief.
BEG_SENT And poor Mr. Darcy!
BEG_SENT Dear Lizzy, only consider what he must have suffered.
BEG_SENT Such a disappointment!
BEG_SENT and with the knowledge of your ill opinion, too!
BEG_SENT and having to relate such a thing of his sister!
BEG_SENT It is really too distressing.
BEG_SENT I am sure you must feel it so.” “Oh!
BEG_SENT no, my regret and compassion are all done away by seeing you so full of both.
BEG_SENT I know you will do him such ample justice, that I am growing every moment more unconcerned and indifferent.
BEG_SENT Your profusion makes me saving; and if you lament over him much longer, my heart will be as light as a feather.” “Poor Wickham!
BEG_SENT there is such an expression of goodness in his countenance!
BEG_SENT such an openness and gentleness in his manner!” “There certainly was some great mismanagement in the education of those two young men.
BEG_SENT One has got all the goodness, and the other all the appearance of it.” “I never thought Mr. Darcy so deficient in the _appearance_ of it as you used to do.” “And yet I meant to be uncommonly clever in taking so decided a dislike to him, without any reason.
BEG_SENT It is such a spur to one’s genius, such an opening for wit, to have a dislike of that kind.
BEG_SENT One may be continually abusive without saying anything just; but one cannot always be laughing at a man without now and then stumbling on something witty.” “Lizzy, when you first read that letter, I am sure you could not treat the matter as you do now.” “Indeed, I could not.
BEG_SENT I was uncomfortable enough, I may say unhappy.
BEG_SENT And with no one to speak to about what I felt, no Jane to comfort me and say that I had not been so very weak and vain and nonsensical as I knew I had!
BEG_SENT Oh!
BEG_SENT how I wanted you!” “How unfortunate that you should have used such very strong expressions in speaking of Wickham to Mr. Darcy, for now they _do_ appear wholly undeserved.” “Certainly.
BEG_SENT But the misfortune of speaking with bitterness is a most natural consequence of the prejudices I had been encouraging.
BEG_SENT There is one point on which I want your advice.
BEG_SENT I want to be told whether I ought, or ought not, to make our acquaintances in general understand Wickham’s character.” Miss Bennet paused a little, and then replied, “Surely there can be no occasion for exposing him so dreadfully.
BEG_SENT What is your opinion?” “That it ought not to be attempted.
BEG_SENT Mr. Darcy has not authorised me to make his communication public.
BEG_SENT On the contrary, every particular relative to his sister was meant to be kept as much as possible to myself; and if I endeavour to undeceive people as to the rest of his conduct, who will believe me?
BEG_SENT The general prejudice against Mr. Darcy is so violent, that it would be the death of half the good people in Meryton to attempt to place him in an amiable light.
BEG_SENT I am not equal to it.
BEG_SENT Wickham will soon be gone; and therefore it will not signify to anyone here what he really is.
BEG_SENT Some time hence it will be all found out, and then we may laugh at their stupidity in not knowing it before.
BEG_SENT At present I will say nothing about it.” “You are quite right.
BEG_SENT To have his errors made public might ruin him for ever.
BEG_SENT He is now, perhaps, sorry for what he has done, and anxious to re-establish a character.
BEG_SENT We must not make him desperate.” The tumult of Elizabeth’s mind was allayed by this conversation.
BEG_SENT She had got rid of two of the secrets which had weighed on her for a fortnight, and was certain of a willing listener in Jane, whenever she might wish to talk again of either.
BEG_SENT But there was still something lurking behind, of which prudence forbade the disclosure.
BEG_SENT She dared not relate the other half of Mr. Darcy’s letter, nor explain to her sister how sincerely she had been valued by her friend.
BEG_SENT Here was knowledge in which no one could partake; and she was sensible that nothing less than a perfect understanding between the parties could justify her in throwing off this last encumbrance of mystery.
BEG_SENT “And then,” said she, “if that very improbable event should ever take place, I shall merely be able to tell what Bingley may tell in a much more agreeable manner himself.
BEG_SENT The liberty of communication cannot be mine till it has lost all its value!” She was now, on being settled at home, at leisure to observe the real state of her sister’s spirits.
BEG_SENT Jane was not happy.
BEG_SENT She still cherished a very tender affection for Bingley.
BEG_SENT Having never even fancied herself in love before, her regard had all the warmth of first attachment, and, from her age and disposition, greater steadiness than most first attachments often boast; and so fervently did she value his remembrance, and prefer him to every other man, that all her good sense, and all her attention to the feelings of her friends, were requisite to check the indulgence of those regrets which must have been injurious to her own health and their tranquillity.
BEG_SENT “Well, Lizzy,” said Mrs. Bennet one day, “what is your opinion _now_ of this sad business of Jane’s?
BEG_SENT For my part, I am determined never to speak of it again to anybody.
BEG_SENT I told my sister Phillips so the other day.
BEG_SENT But I cannot find out that Jane saw anything of him in London.
BEG_SENT Well, he is a very undeserving young man—and I do not suppose there’s the least chance in the world of her ever getting him now.
BEG_SENT There is no talk of his coming to Netherfield again in the summer; and I have inquired of everybody, too, who is likely to know.” “I do not believe he will ever live at Netherfield any more.” “Oh well!
BEG_SENT it is just as he chooses.
BEG_SENT Nobody wants him to come.
BEG_SENT Though I shall always say he used my daughter extremely ill; and if I was her, I would not have put up with it.
BEG_SENT Well, my comfort is, I am sure Jane will die of a broken heart; and then he will be sorry for what he has done.” But as Elizabeth could not receive comfort from any such expectation, she made no answer.
BEG_SENT “Well, Lizzy,” continued her mother, soon afterwards, “and so the Collinses live very comfortable, do they?
BEG_SENT Well, well, I only hope it will last.
BEG_SENT And what sort of table do they keep?
BEG_SENT Charlotte is an excellent manager, I dare say.
BEG_SENT If she is half as sharp as her mother, she is saving enough.
BEG_SENT There is nothing extravagant in _their_ housekeeping, I dare say.” “No, nothing at all.” “A great deal of good management, depend upon it.
BEG_SENT Yes, yes.
BEG_SENT _They_ will take care not to outrun their income.
BEG_SENT _They_ will never be distressed for money.
BEG_SENT Well, much good may it do them!
BEG_SENT And so, I suppose, they often talk of having Longbourn when your father is dead.
BEG_SENT They look upon it as quite their own, I dare say, whenever that happens.” “It was a subject which they could not mention before me.” “No; it would have been strange if they had; but I make no doubt they often talk of it between themselves.
BEG_SENT Well, if they can be easy with an estate that is not lawfully their own, so much the better.
BEG_SENT _I_ should be ashamed of having one that was only entailed on me.”   The first week of their return was soon gone.
BEG_SENT The second began.
BEG_SENT It was the last of the regiment’s stay in Meryton, and all the young ladies in the neighbourhood were drooping apace.
BEG_SENT The dejection was almost universal.
BEG_SENT The elder Miss Bennets alone were still able to eat, drink, and sleep, and pursue the usual course of their employments.
BEG_SENT Very frequently were they reproached for this insensibility by Kitty and Lydia, whose own misery was extreme, and who could not comprehend such hard-heartedness in any of the family.
BEG_SENT “Good Heaven!
BEG_SENT what is to become of us?
BEG_SENT What are we to do?” would they often exclaim in the bitterness of woe.
BEG_SENT “How can you be smiling so, Lizzy?” Their affectionate mother shared all their grief; she remembered what she had herself endured on a similar occasion, five-and-twenty years ago.
BEG_SENT “I am sure,” said she, “I cried for two days together when Colonel Miller’s regiment went away.
BEG_SENT I thought I should have broken my heart.” “I am sure I shall break _mine_,” said Lydia.
BEG_SENT “If one could but go to Brighton!” observed Mrs. Bennet.
BEG_SENT “Oh, yes!—if one could but go to Brighton!
BEG_SENT But papa is so disagreeable.” “A little sea-bathing would set me up forever.” “And my aunt Phillips is sure it would do _me_ a great deal of good,” added Kitty.
BEG_SENT Such were the kind of lamentations resounding perpetually through Longbourn House.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth tried to be diverted by them; but all sense of pleasure was lost in shame.
BEG_SENT She felt anew the justice of Mr. Darcy’s objections; and never had she been so much disposed to pardon his interference in the views of his friend.
BEG_SENT But the gloom of Lydia’s prospect was shortly cleared away; for she received an invitation from Mrs. Forster, the wife of the colonel of the regiment, to accompany her to Brighton.
BEG_SENT This invaluable friend was a very young woman, and very lately married.
BEG_SENT A resemblance in good humour and good spirits had recommended her and Lydia to each other, and out of their _three_ months’ acquaintance they had been intimate _two_.
BEG_SENT The rapture of Lydia on this occasion, her adoration of Mrs. Forster, the delight of Mrs. Bennet, and the mortification of Kitty, are scarcely to be described.
BEG_SENT Wholly inattentive to her sister’s feelings, Lydia flew about the house in restless ecstasy, calling for everyone’s congratulations, and laughing and talking with more violence than ever; whilst the luckless Kitty continued in the parlour repined at her fate in terms as unreasonable as her accent was peevish.
BEG_SENT “I cannot see why Mrs. Forster should not ask _me_ as well as Lydia,” said she, “Though I am _not_ her particular friend.
BEG_SENT I have just as much right to be asked as she has, and more too, for I am two years older.” In vain did Elizabeth attempt to make her reasonable, and Jane to make her resigned.
BEG_SENT As for Elizabeth herself, this invitation was so far from exciting in her the same feelings as in her mother and Lydia, that she considered it as the death warrant of all possibility of common sense for the latter; and detestable as such a step must make her were it known, she could not help secretly advising her father not to let her go.
BEG_SENT She represented to him all the improprieties of Lydia’s general behaviour, the little advantage she could derive from the friendship of such a woman as Mrs. Forster, and the probability of her being yet more imprudent with such a companion at Brighton, where the temptations must be greater than at home.
BEG_SENT He heard her attentively, and then said: “Lydia will never be easy until she has exposed herself in some public place or other, and we can never expect her to do it with so little expense or inconvenience to her family as under the present circumstances.” “If you were aware,” said Elizabeth, “of the very great disadvantage to us all which must arise from the public notice of Lydia’s unguarded and imprudent manner—nay, which has already arisen from it, I am sure you would judge differently in the affair.” “Already arisen?” repeated Mr. Bennet.
BEG_SENT “What, has she frightened away some of your lovers?
BEG_SENT Poor little Lizzy!
BEG_SENT But do not be cast down.
BEG_SENT Such squeamish youths as cannot bear to be connected with a little absurdity are not worth a regret.
BEG_SENT Come, let me see the list of pitiful fellows who have been kept aloof by Lydia’s folly.” “Indeed you are mistaken.
BEG_SENT I have no such injuries to resent.
BEG_SENT It is not of particular, but of general evils, which I am now complaining.
BEG_SENT Our importance, our respectability in the world must be affected by the wild volatility, the assurance and disdain of all restraint which mark Lydia’s character.
BEG_SENT Excuse me, for I must speak plainly.
BEG_SENT If you, my dear father, will not take the trouble of checking her exuberant spirits, and of teaching her that her present pursuits are not to be the business of her life, she will soon be beyond the reach of amendment.
BEG_SENT Her character will be fixed, and she will, at sixteen, be the most determined flirt that ever made herself or her family ridiculous; a flirt, too, in the worst and meanest degree of flirtation; without any attraction beyond youth and a tolerable person; and, from the ignorance and emptiness of her mind, wholly unable to ward off any portion of that universal contempt which her rage for admiration will excite.
BEG_SENT In this danger Kitty also is comprehended.
BEG_SENT She will follow wherever Lydia leads.
BEG_SENT Vain, ignorant, idle, and absolutely uncontrolled!
BEG_SENT Oh!
BEG_SENT my dear father, can you suppose it possible that they will not be censured and despised wherever they are known, and that their sisters will not be often involved in the disgrace?” Mr. Bennet saw that her whole heart was in the subject, and affectionately taking her hand said in reply: “Do not make yourself uneasy, my love.
BEG_SENT Wherever you and Jane are known you must be respected and valued; and you will not appear to less advantage for having a couple of—or I may say, three—very silly sisters.
BEG_SENT We shall have no peace at Longbourn if Lydia does not go to Brighton.
BEG_SENT Let her go, then.
BEG_SENT Colonel Forster is a sensible man, and will keep her out of any real mischief; and she is luckily too poor to be an object of prey to anybody.
BEG_SENT At Brighton she will be of less importance even as a common flirt than she has been here.
BEG_SENT The officers will find women better worth their notice.
BEG_SENT Let us hope, therefore, that her being there may teach her her own insignificance.
BEG_SENT At any rate, she cannot grow many degrees worse, without authorising us to lock her up for the rest of her life.” With this answer Elizabeth was forced to be content; but her own opinion continued the same, and she left him disappointed and sorry.
BEG_SENT It was not in her nature, however, to increase her vexations by dwelling on them.
BEG_SENT She was confident of having performed her duty, and to fret over unavoidable evils, or augment them by anxiety, was no part of her disposition.
BEG_SENT Had Lydia and her mother known the substance of her conference with her father, their indignation would hardly have found expression in their united volubility.
BEG_SENT In Lydia’s imagination, a visit to Brighton comprised every possibility of earthly happiness.
BEG_SENT She saw, with the creative eye of fancy, the streets of that gay bathing-place covered with officers.
BEG_SENT She saw herself the object of attention, to tens and to scores of them at present unknown.
BEG_SENT She saw all the glories of the camp—its tents stretched forth in beauteous uniformity of lines, crowded with the young and the gay, and dazzling with scarlet; and, to complete the view, she saw herself seated beneath a tent, tenderly flirting with at least six officers at once.
BEG_SENT Had she known her sister sought to tear her from such prospects and such realities as these, what would have been her sensations?
BEG_SENT They could have been understood only by her mother, who might have felt nearly the same.
BEG_SENT Lydia’s going to Brighton was all that consoled her for her melancholy conviction of her husband’s never intending to go there himself.
BEG_SENT But they were entirely ignorant of what had passed; and their raptures continued, with little intermission, to the very day of Lydia’s leaving home.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth was now to see Mr. Wickham for the last time.
BEG_SENT Having been frequently in company with him since her return, agitation was pretty well over; the agitations of former partiality entirely so.
BEG_SENT She had even learnt to detect, in the very gentleness which had first delighted her, an affectation and a sameness to disgust and weary.
BEG_SENT In his present behaviour to herself, moreover, she had a fresh source of displeasure, for the inclination he soon testified of renewing those intentions which had marked the early part of their acquaintance could only serve, after what had since passed, to provoke her.
BEG_SENT She lost all concern for him in finding herself thus selected as the object of such idle and frivolous gallantry; and while she steadily repressed it, could not but feel the reproof contained in his believing, that however long, and for whatever cause, his attentions had been withdrawn, her vanity would be gratified, and her preference secured at any time by their renewal.
BEG_SENT On the very last day of the regiment’s remaining at Meryton, he dined, with other of the officers, at Longbourn; and so little was Elizabeth disposed to part from him in good humour, that on his making some inquiry as to the manner in which her time had passed at Hunsford, she mentioned Colonel Fitzwilliam’s and Mr. Darcy’s having both spent three weeks at Rosings, and asked him, if he was acquainted with the former.
BEG_SENT He looked surprised, displeased, alarmed; but with a moment’s recollection and a returning smile, replied, that he had formerly seen him often; and, after observing that he was a very gentlemanlike man, asked her how she had liked him.
BEG_SENT Her answer was warmly in his favour.
BEG_SENT With an air of indifference he soon afterwards added: “How long did you say he was at Rosings?” “Nearly three weeks.” “And you saw him frequently?” “Yes, almost every day.” “His manners are very different from his cousin’s.” “Yes, very different.
BEG_SENT But I think Mr. Darcy improves upon acquaintance.” “Indeed!” cried Mr. Wickham with a look which did not escape her.
BEG_SENT “And pray, may I ask?—” But checking himself, he added, in a gayer tone, “Is it in address that he improves?
BEG_SENT Has he deigned to add aught of civility to his ordinary style?—for I dare not hope,” he continued in a lower and more serious tone, “that he is improved in essentials.” “Oh, no!” said Elizabeth.
BEG_SENT “In essentials, I believe, he is very much what he ever was.” While she spoke, Wickham looked as if scarcely knowing whether to rejoice over her words, or to distrust their meaning.
BEG_SENT There was a something in her countenance which made him listen with an apprehensive and anxious attention, while she added: “When I said that he improved on acquaintance, I did not mean that his mind or his manners were in a state of improvement, but that, from knowing him better, his disposition was better understood.” Wickham’s alarm now appeared in a heightened complexion and agitated look; for a few minutes he was silent, till, shaking off his embarrassment, he turned to her again, and said in the gentlest of accents: “You, who so well know my feeling towards Mr. Darcy, will readily comprehend how sincerely I must rejoice that he is wise enough to assume even the _appearance_ of what is right.
BEG_SENT His pride, in that direction, may be of service, if not to himself, to many others, for it must only deter him from such foul misconduct as I have suffered by.
BEG_SENT I only fear that the sort of cautiousness to which you, I imagine, have been alluding, is merely adopted on his visits to his aunt, of whose good opinion and judgement he stands much in awe.
BEG_SENT His fear of her has always operated, I know, when they were together; and a good deal is to be imputed to his wish of forwarding the match with Miss de Bourgh, which I am certain he has very much at heart.” Elizabeth could not repress a smile at this, but she answered only by a slight inclination of the head.
BEG_SENT She saw that he wanted to engage her on the old subject of his grievances, and she was in no humour to indulge him.
BEG_SENT The rest of the evening passed with the _appearance_, on his side, of usual cheerfulness, but with no further attempt to distinguish Elizabeth; and they parted at last with mutual civility, and possibly a mutual desire of never meeting again.
BEG_SENT When the party broke up, Lydia returned with Mrs. Forster to Meryton, from whence they were to set out early the next morning.
BEG_SENT The separation between her and her family was rather noisy than pathetic.
BEG_SENT Kitty was the only one who shed tears; but she did weep from vexation and envy.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet was diffuse in her good wishes for the felicity of her daughter, and impressive in her injunctions that she should not miss the opportunity of enjoying herself as much as possible—advice which there was every reason to believe would be well attended to; and in the clamorous happiness of Lydia herself in bidding farewell, the more gentle adieus of her sisters were uttered without being heard.
BEG_SENT   Had Elizabeth’s opinion been all drawn from her own family, she could not have formed a very pleasing opinion of conjugal felicity or domestic comfort.
BEG_SENT Her father, captivated by youth and beauty, and that appearance of good humour which youth and beauty generally give, had married a woman whose weak understanding and illiberal mind had very early in their marriage put an end to all real affection for her.
BEG_SENT Respect, esteem, and confidence had vanished for ever; and all his views of domestic happiness were overthrown.
BEG_SENT But Mr. Bennet was not of a disposition to seek comfort for the disappointment which his own imprudence had brought on, in any of those pleasures which too often console the unfortunate for their folly or their vice.
BEG_SENT He was fond of the country and of books; and from these tastes had arisen his principal enjoyments.
BEG_SENT To his wife he was very little otherwise indebted, than as her ignorance and folly had contributed to his amusement.
BEG_SENT This is not the sort of happiness which a man would in general wish to owe to his wife; but where other powers of entertainment are wanting, the true philosopher will derive benefit from such as are given.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth, however, had never been blind to the impropriety of her father’s behaviour as a husband.
BEG_SENT She had always seen it with pain; but respecting his abilities, and grateful for his affectionate treatment of herself, she endeavoured to forget what she could not overlook, and to banish from her thoughts that continual breach of conjugal obligation and decorum which, in exposing his wife to the contempt of her own children, was so highly reprehensible.
BEG_SENT But she had never felt so strongly as now the disadvantages which must attend the children of so unsuitable a marriage, nor ever been so fully aware of the evils arising from so ill-judged a direction of talents; talents, which, rightly used, might at least have preserved the respectability of his daughters, even if incapable of enlarging the mind of his wife.
BEG_SENT When Elizabeth had rejoiced over Wickham’s departure she found little other cause for satisfaction in the loss of the regiment.
BEG_SENT Their parties abroad were less varied than before, and at home she had a mother and sister whose constant repinings at the dullness of everything around them threw a real gloom over their domestic circle; and, though Kitty might in time regain her natural degree of sense, since the disturbers of her brain were removed, her other sister, from whose disposition greater evil might be apprehended, was likely to be hardened in all her folly and assurance by a situation of such double danger as a watering-place and a camp.
BEG_SENT Upon the whole, therefore, she found, what has been sometimes found before, that an event to which she had been looking with impatient desire did not, in taking place, bring all the satisfaction she had promised herself.
BEG_SENT It was consequently necessary to name some other period for the commencement of actual felicity—to have some other point on which her wishes and hopes might be fixed, and by again enjoying the pleasure of anticipation, console herself for the present, and prepare for another disappointment.
BEG_SENT Her tour to the Lakes was now the object of her happiest thoughts; it was her best consolation for all the uncomfortable hours which the discontentedness of her mother and Kitty made inevitable; and could she have included Jane in the scheme, every part of it would have been perfect.
BEG_SENT “But it is fortunate,” thought she, “that I have something to wish for.
BEG_SENT Were the whole arrangement complete, my disappointment would be certain.
BEG_SENT But here, by carrying with me one ceaseless source of regret in my sister’s absence, I may reasonably hope to have all my expectations of pleasure realised.
BEG_SENT A scheme of which every part promises delight can never be successful; and general disappointment is only warded off by the defence of some little peculiar vexation.” When Lydia went away she promised to write very often and very minutely to her mother and Kitty; but her letters were always long expected, and always very short.
BEG_SENT Those to her mother contained little else than that they were just returned from the library, where such and such officers had attended them, and where she had seen such beautiful ornaments as made her quite wild; that she had a new gown, or a new parasol, which she would have described more fully, but was obliged to leave off in a violent hurry, as Mrs. Forster called her, and they were going off to the camp; and from her correspondence with her sister, there was still less to be learnt—for her letters to Kitty, though rather longer, were much too full of lines under the words to be made public.
BEG_SENT After the first fortnight or three weeks of her absence, health, good humour, and cheerfulness began to reappear at Longbourn.
BEG_SENT Everything wore a happier aspect.
BEG_SENT The families who had been in town for the winter came back again, and summer finery and summer engagements arose.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet was restored to her usual querulous serenity; and, by the middle of June, Kitty was so much recovered as to be able to enter Meryton without tears; an event of such happy promise as to make Elizabeth hope that by the following Christmas she might be so tolerably reasonable as not to mention an officer above once a day, unless, by some cruel and malicious arrangement at the War Office, another regiment should be quartered in Meryton.
BEG_SENT The time fixed for the beginning of their northern tour was now fast approaching, and a fortnight only was wanting of it, when a letter arrived from Mrs. Gardiner, which at once delayed its commencement and curtailed its extent.
BEG_SENT Mr. Gardiner would be prevented by business from setting out till a fortnight later in July, and must be in London again within a month, and as that left too short a period for them to go so far, and see so much as they had proposed, or at least to see it with the leisure and comfort they had built on, they were obliged to give up the Lakes, and substitute a more contracted tour, and, according to the present plan, were to go no farther northwards than Derbyshire.
BEG_SENT In that county there was enough to be seen to occupy the chief of their three weeks; and to Mrs. Gardiner it had a peculiarly strong attraction.
BEG_SENT The town where she had formerly passed some years of her life, and where they were now to spend a few days, was probably as great an object of her curiosity as all the celebrated beauties of Matlock, Chatsworth, Dovedale, or the Peak.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth was excessively disappointed; she had set her heart on seeing the Lakes, and still thought there might have been time enough.
BEG_SENT But it was her business to be satisfied—and certainly her temper to be happy; and all was soon right again.
BEG_SENT With the mention of Derbyshire there were many ideas connected.
BEG_SENT It was impossible for her to see the word without thinking of Pemberley and its owner.
BEG_SENT “But surely,” said she, “I may enter his county with impunity, and rob it of a few petrified spars without his perceiving me.” The period of expectation was now doubled.
BEG_SENT Four weeks were to pass away before her uncle and aunt’s arrival.
BEG_SENT But they did pass away, and Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner, with their four children, did at length appear at Longbourn.
BEG_SENT The children, two girls of six and eight years old, and two younger boys, were to be left under the particular care of their cousin Jane, who was the general favourite, and whose steady sense and sweetness of temper exactly adapted her for attending to them in every way—teaching them, playing with them, and loving them.
BEG_SENT The Gardiners stayed only one night at Longbourn, and set off the next morning with Elizabeth in pursuit of novelty and amusement.
BEG_SENT One enjoyment was certain—that of suitableness of companions; a suitableness which comprehended health and temper to bear inconveniences—cheerfulness to enhance every pleasure—and affection and intelligence, which might supply it among themselves if there were disappointments abroad.
BEG_SENT It is not the object of this work to give a description of Derbyshire, nor of any of the remarkable places through which their route thither lay; Oxford, Blenheim, Warwick, Kenilworth, Birmingham, etc.
BEG_SENT are sufficiently known.
BEG_SENT A small part of Derbyshire is all the present concern.
BEG_SENT To the little town of Lambton, the scene of Mrs. Gardiner’s former residence, and where she had lately learned some acquaintance still remained, they bent their steps, after having seen all the principal wonders of the country; and within five miles of Lambton, Elizabeth found from her aunt that Pemberley was situated.
BEG_SENT It was not in their direct road, nor more than a mile or two out of it.
BEG_SENT In talking over their route the evening before, Mrs. Gardiner expressed an inclination to see the place again.
BEG_SENT Mr. Gardiner declared his willingness, and Elizabeth was applied to for her approbation.
BEG_SENT “My love, should not you like to see a place of which you have heard so much?” said her aunt; “a place, too, with which so many of your acquaintances are connected.
BEG_SENT Wickham passed all his youth there, you know.” Elizabeth was distressed.
BEG_SENT She felt that she had no business at Pemberley, and was obliged to assume a disinclination for seeing it.
BEG_SENT She must own that she was tired of seeing great houses; after going over so many, she really had no pleasure in fine carpets or satin curtains.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Gardiner abused her stupidity.
BEG_SENT “If it were merely a fine house richly furnished,” said she, “I should not care about it myself; but the grounds are delightful.
BEG_SENT They have some of the finest woods in the country.” Elizabeth said no more—but her mind could not acquiesce.
BEG_SENT The possibility of meeting Mr. Darcy, while viewing the place, instantly occurred.
BEG_SENT It would be dreadful!
BEG_SENT She blushed at the very idea, and thought it would be better to speak openly to her aunt than to run such a risk.
BEG_SENT But against this there were objections; and she finally resolved that it could be the last resource, if her private inquiries to the absence of the family were unfavourably answered.
BEG_SENT Accordingly, when she retired at night, she asked the chambermaid whether Pemberley were not a very fine place?
BEG_SENT what was the name of its proprietor?
BEG_SENT and, with no little alarm, whether the family were down for the summer?
BEG_SENT A most welcome negative followed the last question—and her alarms now being removed, she was at leisure to feel a great deal of curiosity to see the house herself; and when the subject was revived the next morning, and she was again applied to, could readily answer, and with a proper air of indifference, that she had not really any dislike to the scheme.
BEG_SENT To Pemberley, therefore, they were to go.
BEG_SENT   Elizabeth, as they drove along, watched for the first appearance of Pemberley Woods with some perturbation; and when at length they turned in at the lodge, her spirits were in a high flutter.
BEG_SENT The park was very large, and contained great variety of ground.
BEG_SENT They entered it in one of its lowest points, and drove for some time through a beautiful wood stretching over a wide extent.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth’s mind was too full for conversation, but she saw and admired every remarkable spot and point of view.
BEG_SENT They gradually ascended for half-a-mile, and then found themselves at the top of a considerable eminence, where the wood ceased, and the eye was instantly caught by Pemberley House, situated on the opposite side of a valley, into which the road with some abruptness wound.
BEG_SENT It was a large, handsome stone building, standing well on rising ground, and backed by a ridge of high woody hills; and in front, a stream of some natural importance was swelled into greater, but without any artificial appearance.
BEG_SENT Its banks were neither formal nor falsely adorned.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth was delighted.
BEG_SENT She had never seen a place for which nature had done more, or where natural beauty had been so little counteracted by an awkward taste.
BEG_SENT They were all of them warm in their admiration; and at that moment she felt that to be mistress of Pemberley might be something!
BEG_SENT They descended the hill, crossed the bridge, and drove to the door; and, while examining the nearer aspect of the house, all her apprehension of meeting its owner returned.
BEG_SENT She dreaded lest the chambermaid had been mistaken.
BEG_SENT On applying to see the place, they were admitted into the hall; and Elizabeth, as they waited for the housekeeper, had leisure to wonder at her being where she was.
BEG_SENT The housekeeper came; a respectable-looking elderly woman, much less fine, and more civil, than she had any notion of finding her.
BEG_SENT They followed her into the dining-parlour.
BEG_SENT It was a large, well proportioned room, handsomely fitted up.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth, after slightly surveying it, went to a window to enjoy its prospect.
BEG_SENT The hill, crowned with wood, which they had descended, receiving increased abruptness from the distance, was a beautiful object.
BEG_SENT Every disposition of the ground was good; and she looked on the whole scene, the river, the trees scattered on its banks and the winding of the valley, as far as she could trace it, with delight.
BEG_SENT As they passed into other rooms these objects were taking different positions; but from every window there were beauties to be seen.
BEG_SENT The rooms were lofty and handsome, and their furniture suitable to the fortune of its proprietor; but Elizabeth saw, with admiration of his taste, that it was neither gaudy nor uselessly fine; with less of splendour, and more real elegance, than the furniture of Rosings.
BEG_SENT “And of this place,” thought she, “I might have been mistress!
BEG_SENT With these rooms I might now have been familiarly acquainted!
BEG_SENT Instead of viewing them as a stranger, I might have rejoiced in them as my own, and welcomed to them as visitors my uncle and aunt.
BEG_SENT But no,”—recollecting herself—“that could never be; my uncle and aunt would have been lost to me; I should not have been allowed to invite them.” This was a lucky recollection—it saved her from something very like regret.
BEG_SENT She longed to inquire of the housekeeper whether her master was really absent, but had not the courage for it.
BEG_SENT At length however, the question was asked by her uncle; and she turned away with alarm, while Mrs. Reynolds replied that he was, adding, “But we expect him to-morrow, with a large party of friends.” How rejoiced was Elizabeth that their own journey had not by any circumstance been delayed a day!
BEG_SENT Her aunt now called her to look at a picture.
BEG_SENT She approached and saw the likeness of Mr. Wickham, suspended, amongst several other miniatures, over the mantelpiece.
BEG_SENT Her aunt asked her, smilingly, how she liked it.
BEG_SENT The housekeeper came forward, and told them it was a picture of a young gentleman, the son of her late master’s steward, who had been brought up by him at his own expense.
BEG_SENT “He is now gone into the army,” she added; “but I am afraid he has turned out very wild.” Mrs. Gardiner looked at her niece with a smile, but Elizabeth could not return it.
BEG_SENT “And that,” said Mrs. Reynolds, pointing to another of the miniatures, “is my master—and very like him.
BEG_SENT It was drawn at the same time as the other—about eight years ago.” “I have heard much of your master’s fine person,” said Mrs. Gardiner, looking at the picture; “it is a handsome face.
BEG_SENT But, Lizzy, you can tell us whether it is like or not.” Mrs. Reynolds respect for Elizabeth seemed to increase on this intimation of her knowing her master.
BEG_SENT “Does that young lady know Mr. Darcy?” Elizabeth coloured, and said: “A little.” “And do not you think him a very handsome gentleman, ma’am?” “Yes, very handsome.” “I am sure _I_ know none so handsome; but in the gallery up stairs you will see a finer, larger picture of him than this.
BEG_SENT This room was my late master’s favourite room, and these miniatures are just as they used to be then.
BEG_SENT He was very fond of them.” This accounted to Elizabeth for Mr. Wickham’s being among them.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Reynolds then directed their attention to one of Miss Darcy, drawn when she was only eight years old.
BEG_SENT “And is Miss Darcy as handsome as her brother?” said Mrs. Gardiner.
BEG_SENT “Oh!
BEG_SENT yes—the handsomest young lady that ever was seen; and so accomplished!—She plays and sings all day long.
BEG_SENT In the next room is a new instrument just come down for her—a present from my master; she comes here to-morrow with him.” Mr. Gardiner, whose manners were very easy and pleasant, encouraged her communicativeness by his questions and remarks; Mrs. Reynolds, either by pride or attachment, had evidently great pleasure in talking of her master and his sister.
BEG_SENT “Is your master much at Pemberley in the course of the year?” “Not so much as I could wish, sir; but I dare say he may spend half his time here; and Miss Darcy is always down for the summer months.” “Except,” thought Elizabeth, “when she goes to Ramsgate.” “If your master would marry, you might see more of him.” “Yes, sir; but I do not know when _that_ will be.
BEG_SENT I do not know who is good enough for him.” Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner smiled.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth could not help saying, “It is very much to his credit, I am sure, that you should think so.” “I say no more than the truth, and everybody will say that knows him,” replied the other.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth thought this was going pretty far; and she listened with increasing astonishment as the housekeeper added, “I have never known a cross word from him in my life, and I have known him ever since he was four years old.” This was praise, of all others most extraordinary, most opposite to her ideas.
BEG_SENT That he was not a good-tempered man had been her firmest opinion.
BEG_SENT Her keenest attention was awakened; she longed to hear more, and was grateful to her uncle for saying: “There are very few people of whom so much can be said.
BEG_SENT You are lucky in having such a master.” “Yes, sir, I know I am.
BEG_SENT If I were to go through the world, I could not meet with a better.
BEG_SENT But I have always observed, that they who are good-natured when children, are good-natured when they grow up; and he was always the sweetest-tempered, most generous-hearted boy in the world.” Elizabeth almost stared at her.
BEG_SENT “Can this be Mr. Darcy?” thought she.
BEG_SENT “His father was an excellent man,” said Mrs. Gardiner.
BEG_SENT “Yes, ma’am, that he was indeed; and his son will be just like him—just as affable to the poor.” Elizabeth listened, wondered, doubted, and was impatient for more.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Reynolds could interest her on no other point.
BEG_SENT She related the subjects of the pictures, the dimensions of the rooms, and the price of the furniture, in vain.
BEG_SENT Mr. Gardiner, highly amused by the kind of family prejudice to which he attributed her excessive commendation of her master, soon led again to the subject; and she dwelt with energy on his many merits as they proceeded together up the great staircase.
BEG_SENT “He is the best landlord, and the best master,” said she, “that ever lived; not like the wild young men nowadays, who think of nothing but themselves.
BEG_SENT There is not one of his tenants or servants but will give him a good name.
BEG_SENT Some people call him proud; but I am sure I never saw anything of it.
BEG_SENT To my fancy, it is only because he does not rattle away like other young men.” “In what an amiable light does this place him!” thought Elizabeth.
BEG_SENT “This fine account of him,” whispered her aunt as they walked, “is not quite consistent with his behaviour to our poor friend.” “Perhaps we might be deceived.” “That is not very likely; our authority was too good.” On reaching the spacious lobby above they were shown into a very pretty sitting-room, lately fitted up with greater elegance and lightness than the apartments below; and were informed that it was but just done to give pleasure to Miss Darcy, who had taken a liking to the room when last at Pemberley.
BEG_SENT “He is certainly a good brother,” said Elizabeth, as she walked towards one of the windows.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Reynolds anticipated Miss Darcy’s delight, when she should enter the room.
BEG_SENT “And this is always the way with him,” she added.
BEG_SENT “Whatever can give his sister any pleasure is sure to be done in a moment.
BEG_SENT There is nothing he would not do for her.” The picture-gallery, and two or three of the principal bedrooms, were all that remained to be shown.
BEG_SENT In the former were many good paintings; but Elizabeth knew nothing of the art; and from such as had been already visible below, she had willingly turned to look at some drawings of Miss Darcy’s, in crayons, whose subjects were usually more interesting, and also more intelligible.
BEG_SENT In the gallery there were many family portraits, but they could have little to fix the attention of a stranger.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth walked in quest of the only face whose features would be known to her.
BEG_SENT At last it arrested her—and she beheld a striking resemblance to Mr. Darcy, with such a smile over the face as she remembered to have sometimes seen when he looked at her.
BEG_SENT She stood several minutes before the picture, in earnest contemplation, and returned to it again before they quitted the gallery.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Reynolds informed them that it had been taken in his father’s lifetime.
BEG_SENT There was certainly at this moment, in Elizabeth’s mind, a more gentle sensation towards the original than she had ever felt at the height of their acquaintance.
BEG_SENT The commendation bestowed on him by Mrs. Reynolds was of no trifling nature.
BEG_SENT What praise is more valuable than the praise of an intelligent servant?
BEG_SENT As a brother, a landlord, a master, she considered how many people’s happiness were in his guardianship!—how much of pleasure or pain was it in his power to bestow!—how much of good or evil must be done by him!
BEG_SENT Every idea that had been brought forward by the housekeeper was favourable to his character, and as she stood before the canvas on which he was represented, and fixed his eyes upon herself, she thought of his regard with a deeper sentiment of gratitude than it had ever raised before; she remembered its warmth, and softened its impropriety of expression.
BEG_SENT When all of the house that was open to general inspection had been seen, they returned downstairs, and, taking leave of the housekeeper, were consigned over to the gardener, who met them at the hall-door.
BEG_SENT As they walked across the hall towards the river, Elizabeth turned back to look again; her uncle and aunt stopped also, and while the former was conjecturing as to the date of the building, the owner of it himself suddenly came forward from the road, which led behind it to the stables.
BEG_SENT They were within twenty yards of each other, and so abrupt was his appearance, that it was impossible to avoid his sight.
BEG_SENT Their eyes instantly met, and the cheeks of both were overspread with the deepest blush.
BEG_SENT He absolutely started, and for a moment seemed immovable from surprise; but shortly recovering himself, advanced towards the party, and spoke to Elizabeth, if not in terms of perfect composure, at least of perfect civility.
BEG_SENT She had instinctively turned away; but stopping on his approach, received his compliments with an embarrassment impossible to be overcome.
BEG_SENT Had his first appearance, or his resemblance to the picture they had just been examining, been insufficient to assure the other two that they now saw Mr. Darcy, the gardener’s expression of surprise, on beholding his master, must immediately have told it.
BEG_SENT They stood a little aloof while he was talking to their niece, who, astonished and confused, scarcely dared lift her eyes to his face, and knew not what answer she returned to his civil inquiries after her family.
BEG_SENT Amazed at the alteration of his manner since they last parted, every sentence that he uttered was increasing her embarrassment; and every idea of the impropriety of her being found there recurring to her mind, the few minutes in which they continued were some of the most uncomfortable in her life.
BEG_SENT Nor did he seem much more at ease; when he spoke, his accent had none of its usual sedateness; and he repeated his inquiries as to the time of her having left Longbourn, and of her having stayed in Derbyshire, so often, and in so hurried a way, as plainly spoke the distraction of his thoughts.
BEG_SENT At length every idea seemed to fail him; and, after standing a few moments without saying a word, he suddenly recollected himself, and took leave.
BEG_SENT The others then joined her, and expressed admiration of his figure; but Elizabeth heard not a word, and wholly engrossed by her own feelings, followed them in silence.
BEG_SENT She was overpowered by shame and vexation.
BEG_SENT Her coming there was the most unfortunate, the most ill-judged thing in the world!
BEG_SENT How strange it must appear to him!
BEG_SENT In what a disgraceful light might it not strike so vain a man!
BEG_SENT It might seem as if she had purposely thrown herself in his way again!
BEG_SENT Oh!
BEG_SENT why did she come?
BEG_SENT Or, why did he thus come a day before he was expected?
BEG_SENT Had they been only ten minutes sooner, they should have been beyond the reach of his discrimination; for it was plain that he was that moment arrived—that moment alighted from his horse or his carriage.
BEG_SENT She blushed again and again over the perverseness of the meeting.
BEG_SENT And his behaviour, so strikingly altered—what could it mean?
BEG_SENT That he should even speak to her was amazing!—but to speak with such civility, to inquire after her family!
BEG_SENT Never in her life had she seen his manners so little dignified, never had he spoken with such gentleness as on this unexpected meeting.
BEG_SENT What a contrast did it offer to his last address in Rosings Park, when he put his letter into her hand!
BEG_SENT She knew not what to think, or how to account for it.
BEG_SENT They had now entered a beautiful walk by the side of the water, and every step was bringing forward a nobler fall of ground, or a finer reach of the woods to which they were approaching; but it was some time before Elizabeth was sensible of any of it; and, though she answered mechanically to the repeated appeals of her uncle and aunt, and seemed to direct her eyes to such objects as they pointed out, she distinguished no part of the scene.
BEG_SENT Her thoughts were all fixed on that one spot of Pemberley House, whichever it might be, where Mr. Darcy then was.
BEG_SENT She longed to know what at the moment was passing in his mind—in what manner he thought of her, and whether, in defiance of everything, she was still dear to him.
BEG_SENT Perhaps he had been civil only because he felt himself at ease; yet there had been _that_ in his voice which was not like ease.
BEG_SENT Whether he had felt more of pain or of pleasure in seeing her she could not tell, but he certainly had not seen her with composure.
BEG_SENT At length, however, the remarks of her companions on her absence of mind aroused her, and she felt the necessity of appearing more like herself.
BEG_SENT They entered the woods, and bidding adieu to the river for a while, ascended some of the higher grounds; when, in spots where the opening of the trees gave the eye power to wander, were many charming views of the valley, the opposite hills, with the long range of woods overspreading many, and occasionally part of the stream.
BEG_SENT Mr. Gardiner expressed a wish of going round the whole park, but feared it might be beyond a walk.
BEG_SENT With a triumphant smile they were told that it was ten miles round.
BEG_SENT It settled the matter; and they pursued the accustomed circuit; which brought them again, after some time, in a descent among hanging woods, to the edge of the water, and one of its narrowest parts.
BEG_SENT They crossed it by a simple bridge, in character with the general air of the scene; it was a spot less adorned than any they had yet visited; and the valley, here contracted into a glen, allowed room only for the stream, and a narrow walk amidst the rough coppice-wood which bordered it.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth longed to explore its windings; but when they had crossed the bridge, and perceived their distance from the house, Mrs. Gardiner, who was not a great walker, could go no farther, and thought only of returning to the carriage as quickly as possible.
BEG_SENT Her niece was, therefore, obliged to submit, and they took their way towards the house on the opposite side of the river, in the nearest direction; but their progress was slow, for Mr. Gardiner, though seldom able to indulge the taste, was very fond of fishing, and was so much engaged in watching the occasional appearance of some trout in the water, and talking to the man about them, that he advanced but little.
BEG_SENT Whilst wandering on in this slow manner, they were again surprised, and Elizabeth’s astonishment was quite equal to what it had been at first, by the sight of Mr. Darcy approaching them, and at no great distance.
BEG_SENT The walk here being here less sheltered than on the other side, allowed them to see him before they met.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth, however astonished, was at least more prepared for an interview than before, and resolved to appear and to speak with calmness, if he really intended to meet them.
BEG_SENT For a few moments, indeed, she felt that he would probably strike into some other path.
BEG_SENT The idea lasted while a turning in the walk concealed him from their view; the turning past, he was immediately before them.
BEG_SENT With a glance, she saw that he had lost none of his recent civility; and, to imitate his politeness, she began, as they met, to admire the beauty of the place; but she had not got beyond the words “delightful,” and “charming,” when some unlucky recollections obtruded, and she fancied that praise of Pemberley from her might be mischievously construed.
BEG_SENT Her colour changed, and she said no more.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Gardiner was standing a little behind; and on her pausing, he asked her if she would do him the honour of introducing him to her friends.
BEG_SENT This was a stroke of civility for which she was quite unprepared; and she could hardly suppress a smile at his being now seeking the acquaintance of some of those very people against whom his pride had revolted in his offer to herself.
BEG_SENT “What will be his surprise,” thought she, “when he knows who they are?
BEG_SENT He takes them now for people of fashion.” The introduction, however, was immediately made; and as she named their relationship to herself, she stole a sly look at him, to see how he bore it, and was not without the expectation of his decamping as fast as he could from such disgraceful companions.
BEG_SENT That he was _surprised_ by the connection was evident; he sustained it, however, with fortitude, and so far from going away, turned back with them, and entered into conversation with Mr. Gardiner.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth could not but be pleased, could not but triumph.
BEG_SENT It was consoling that he should know she had some relations for whom there was no need to blush.
BEG_SENT She listened most attentively to all that passed between them, and gloried in every expression, every sentence of her uncle, which marked his intelligence, his taste, or his good manners.
BEG_SENT The conversation soon turned upon fishing; and she heard Mr. Darcy invite him, with the greatest civility, to fish there as often as he chose while he continued in the neighbourhood, offering at the same time to supply him with fishing tackle, and pointing out those parts of the stream where there was usually most sport.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Gardiner, who was walking arm-in-arm with Elizabeth, gave her a look expressive of wonder.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth said nothing, but it gratified her exceedingly; the compliment must be all for herself.
BEG_SENT Her astonishment, however, was extreme, and continually was she repeating, “Why is he so altered?
BEG_SENT From what can it proceed?
BEG_SENT It cannot be for _me_—it cannot be for _my_ sake that his manners are thus softened.
BEG_SENT My reproofs at Hunsford could not work such a change as this.
BEG_SENT It is impossible that he should still love me.” After walking some time in this way, the two ladies in front, the two gentlemen behind, on resuming their places, after descending to the brink of the river for the better inspection of some curious water-plant, there chanced to be a little alteration.
BEG_SENT It originated in Mrs. Gardiner, who, fatigued by the exercise of the morning, found Elizabeth’s arm inadequate to her support, and consequently preferred her husband’s.
BEG_SENT Mr. Darcy took her place by her niece, and they walked on together.
BEG_SENT After a short silence, the lady first spoke.
BEG_SENT She wished him to know that she had been assured of his absence before she came to the place, and accordingly began by observing, that his arrival had been very unexpected—“for your housekeeper,” she added, “informed us that you would certainly not be here till to-morrow; and indeed, before we left Bakewell, we understood that you were not immediately expected in the country.” He acknowledged the truth of it all, and said that business with his steward had occasioned his coming forward a few hours before the rest of the party with whom he had been travelling.
BEG_SENT “They will join me early to-morrow,” he continued, “and among them are some who will claim an acquaintance with you—Mr.
BEG_SENT Bingley and his sisters.” Elizabeth answered only by a slight bow.
BEG_SENT Her thoughts were instantly driven back to the time when Mr. Bingley’s name had been the last mentioned between them; and, if she might judge by his complexion, _his_ mind was not very differently engaged.
BEG_SENT “There is also one other person in the party,” he continued after a pause, “who more particularly wishes to be known to you.
BEG_SENT Will you allow me, or do I ask too much, to introduce my sister to your acquaintance during your stay at Lambton?” The surprise of such an application was great indeed; it was too great for her to know in what manner she acceded to it.
BEG_SENT She immediately felt that whatever desire Miss Darcy might have of being acquainted with her must be the work of her brother, and, without looking farther, it was satisfactory; it was gratifying to know that his resentment had not made him think really ill of her.
BEG_SENT They now walked on in silence, each of them deep in thought.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth was not comfortable; that was impossible; but she was flattered and pleased.
BEG_SENT His wish of introducing his sister to her was a compliment of the highest kind.
BEG_SENT They soon outstripped the others, and when they had reached the carriage, Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner were half a quarter of a mile behind.
BEG_SENT He then asked her to walk into the house—but she declared herself not tired, and they stood together on the lawn.
BEG_SENT At such a time much might have been said, and silence was very awkward.
BEG_SENT She wanted to talk, but there seemed to be an embargo on every subject.
BEG_SENT At last she recollected that she had been travelling, and they talked of Matlock and Dove Dale with great perseverance.
BEG_SENT Yet time and her aunt moved slowly—and her patience and her ideas were nearly worn out before the _tête-à-tête_ was over.
BEG_SENT On Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner’s coming up they were all pressed to go into the house and take some refreshment; but this was declined, and they parted on each side with utmost politeness.
BEG_SENT Mr. Darcy handed the ladies into the carriage; and when it drove off, Elizabeth saw him walking slowly towards the house.
BEG_SENT The observations of her uncle and aunt now began; and each of them pronounced him to be infinitely superior to anything they had expected.
BEG_SENT “He is perfectly well behaved, polite, and unassuming,” said her uncle.
BEG_SENT “There _is_ something a little stately in him, to be sure,” replied her aunt, “but it is confined to his air, and is not unbecoming.
BEG_SENT I can now say with the housekeeper, that though some people may call him proud, _I_ have seen nothing of it.” “I was never more surprised than by his behaviour to us.
BEG_SENT It was more than civil; it was really attentive; and there was no necessity for such attention.
BEG_SENT His acquaintance with Elizabeth was very trifling.” “To be sure, Lizzy,” said her aunt, “he is not so handsome as Wickham; or, rather, he has not Wickham’s countenance, for his features are perfectly good.
BEG_SENT But how came you to tell me that he was so disagreeable?” Elizabeth excused herself as well as she could; said that she had liked him better when they had met in Kent than before, and that she had never seen him so pleasant as this morning.
BEG_SENT “But perhaps he may be a little whimsical in his civilities,” replied her uncle.
BEG_SENT “Your great men often are; and therefore I shall not take him at his word, as he might change his mind another day, and warn me off his grounds.” Elizabeth felt that they had entirely misunderstood his character, but said nothing.
BEG_SENT “From what we have seen of him,” continued Mrs. Gardiner, “I really should not have thought that he could have behaved in so cruel a way by anybody as he has done by poor Wickham.
BEG_SENT He has not an ill-natured look.
BEG_SENT On the contrary, there is something pleasing about his mouth when he speaks.
BEG_SENT And there is something of dignity in his countenance that would not give one an unfavourable idea of his heart.
BEG_SENT But, to be sure, the good lady who showed us his house did give him a most flaming character!
BEG_SENT I could hardly help laughing aloud sometimes.
BEG_SENT But he is a liberal master, I suppose, and _that_ in the eye of a servant comprehends every virtue.” Elizabeth here felt herself called on to say something in vindication of his behaviour to Wickham; and therefore gave them to understand, in as guarded a manner as she could, that by what she had heard from his relations in Kent, his actions were capable of a very different construction; and that his character was by no means so faulty, nor Wickham’s so amiable, as they had been considered in Hertfordshire.
BEG_SENT In confirmation of this, she related the particulars of all the pecuniary transactions in which they had been connected, without actually naming her authority, but stating it to be such as might be relied on.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Gardiner was surprised and concerned; but as they were now approaching the scene of her former pleasures, every idea gave way to the charm of recollection; and she was too much engaged in pointing out to her husband all the interesting spots in its environs to think of anything else.
BEG_SENT Fatigued as she had been by the morning’s walk they had no sooner dined than she set off again in quest of her former acquaintance, and the evening was spent in the satisfactions of an intercourse renewed after many years’ discontinuance.
BEG_SENT The occurrences of the day were too full of interest to leave Elizabeth much attention for any of these new friends; and she could do nothing but think, and think with wonder, of Mr. Darcy’s civility, and, above all, of his wishing her to be acquainted with his sister.
BEG_SENT   Elizabeth had settled it that Mr. Darcy would bring his sister to visit her the very day after her reaching Pemberley; and was consequently resolved not to be out of sight of the inn the whole of that morning.
BEG_SENT But her conclusion was false; for on the very morning after their arrival at Lambton, these visitors came.
BEG_SENT They had been walking about the place with some of their new friends, and were just returning to the inn to dress themselves for dining with the same family, when the sound of a carriage drew them to a window, and they saw a gentleman and a lady in a curricle driving up the street.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth immediately recognizing the livery, guessed what it meant, and imparted no small degree of her surprise to her relations by acquainting them with the honour which she expected.
BEG_SENT Her uncle and aunt were all amazement; and the embarrassment of her manner as she spoke, joined to the circumstance itself, and many of the circumstances of the preceding day, opened to them a new idea on the business.
BEG_SENT Nothing had ever suggested it before, but they felt that there was no other way of accounting for such attentions from such a quarter than by supposing a partiality for their niece.
BEG_SENT While these newly-born notions were passing in their heads, the perturbation of Elizabeth’s feelings was at every moment increasing.
BEG_SENT She was quite amazed at her own discomposure; but amongst other causes of disquiet, she dreaded lest the partiality of the brother should have said too much in her favour; and, more than commonly anxious to please, she naturally suspected that every power of pleasing would fail her.
BEG_SENT She retreated from the window, fearful of being seen; and as she walked up and down the room, endeavouring to compose herself, saw such looks of inquiring surprise in her uncle and aunt as made everything worse.
BEG_SENT Miss Darcy and her brother appeared, and this formidable introduction took place.
BEG_SENT With astonishment did Elizabeth see that her new acquaintance was at least as much embarrassed as herself.
BEG_SENT Since her being at Lambton, she had heard that Miss Darcy was exceedingly proud; but the observation of a very few minutes convinced her that she was only exceedingly shy.
BEG_SENT She found it difficult to obtain even a word from her beyond a monosyllable.
BEG_SENT Miss Darcy was tall, and on a larger scale than Elizabeth; and, though little more than sixteen, her figure was formed, and her appearance womanly and graceful.
BEG_SENT She was less handsome than her brother; but there was sense and good humour in her face, and her manners were perfectly unassuming and gentle.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth, who had expected to find in her as acute and unembarrassed an observer as ever Mr. Darcy had been, was much relieved by discerning such different feelings.
BEG_SENT They had not long been together before Mr. Darcy told her that Bingley was also coming to wait on her; and she had barely time to express her satisfaction, and prepare for such a visitor, when Bingley’s quick step was heard on the stairs, and in a moment he entered the room.
BEG_SENT All Elizabeth’s anger against him had been long done away; but had she still felt any, it could hardly have stood its ground against the unaffected cordiality with which he expressed himself on seeing her again.
BEG_SENT He inquired in a friendly, though general way, after her family, and looked and spoke with the same good-humoured ease that he had ever done.
BEG_SENT To Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner he was scarcely a less interesting personage than to herself.
BEG_SENT They had long wished to see him.
BEG_SENT The whole party before them, indeed, excited a lively attention.
BEG_SENT The suspicions which had just arisen of Mr. Darcy and their niece directed their observation towards each with an earnest though guarded inquiry; and they soon drew from those inquiries the full conviction that one of them at least knew what it was to love.
BEG_SENT Of the lady’s sensations they remained a little in doubt; but that the gentleman was overflowing with admiration was evident enough.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth, on her side, had much to do.
BEG_SENT She wanted to ascertain the feelings of each of her visitors; she wanted to compose her own, and to make herself agreeable to all; and in the latter object, where she feared most to fail, she was most sure of success, for those to whom she endeavoured to give pleasure were prepossessed in her favour.
BEG_SENT Bingley was ready, Georgiana was eager, and Darcy determined, to be pleased.
BEG_SENT In seeing Bingley, her thoughts naturally flew to her sister; and, oh!
BEG_SENT how ardently did she long to know whether any of his were directed in a like manner.
BEG_SENT Sometimes she could fancy that he talked less than on former occasions, and once or twice pleased herself with the notion that, as he looked at her, he was trying to trace a resemblance.
