BEG_SENT But there are two things that I want very much to know; one is, how much money your uncle has laid down to bring it about; and the other, how am I ever to pay him.” “Money!
BEG_SENT My uncle!” cried Jane, “what do you mean, sir?” “I mean, that no man in his senses would marry Lydia on so slight a temptation as one hundred a year during my life, and fifty after I am gone.” “That is very true,” said Elizabeth; “though it had not occurred to me before.
BEG_SENT His debts to be discharged, and something still to remain!
BEG_SENT Oh!
BEG_SENT it must be my uncle’s doings!
BEG_SENT Generous, good man, I am afraid he has distressed himself.
BEG_SENT A small sum could not do all this.” “No,” said her father; “Wickham’s a fool if he takes her with a farthing less than ten thousand pounds.
BEG_SENT I should be sorry to think so ill of him, in the very beginning of our relationship.” “Ten thousand pounds!
BEG_SENT Heaven forbid!
BEG_SENT How is half such a sum to be repaid?” Mr. Bennet made no answer, and each of them, deep in thought, continued silent till they reached the house.
BEG_SENT Their father then went on to the library to write, and the girls walked into the breakfast-room.
BEG_SENT “And they are really to be married!” cried Elizabeth, as soon as they were by themselves.
BEG_SENT “How strange this is!
BEG_SENT And for _this_ we are to be thankful.
BEG_SENT That they should marry, small as is their chance of happiness, and wretched as is his character, we are forced to rejoice.
BEG_SENT Oh, Lydia!” “I comfort myself with thinking,” replied Jane, “that he certainly would not marry Lydia if he had not a real regard for her.
BEG_SENT Though our kind uncle has done something towards clearing him, I cannot believe that ten thousand pounds, or anything like it, has been advanced.
BEG_SENT He has children of his own, and may have more.
BEG_SENT How could he spare half ten thousand pounds?” “If he were ever able to learn what Wickham’s debts have been,” said Elizabeth, “and how much is settled on his side on our sister, we shall exactly know what Mr. Gardiner has done for them, because Wickham has not sixpence of his own.
BEG_SENT The kindness of my uncle and aunt can never be requited.
BEG_SENT Their taking her home, and affording her their personal protection and countenance, is such a sacrifice to her advantage as years of gratitude cannot enough acknowledge.
BEG_SENT By this time she is actually with them!
BEG_SENT If such goodness does not make her miserable now, she will never deserve to be happy!
BEG_SENT What a meeting for her, when she first sees my aunt!” “We must endeavour to forget all that has passed on either side,” said Jane: “I hope and trust they will yet be happy.
BEG_SENT His consenting to marry her is a proof, I will believe, that he is come to a right way of thinking.
BEG_SENT Their mutual affection will steady them; and I flatter myself they will settle so quietly, and live in so rational a manner, as may in time make their past imprudence forgotten.” “Their conduct has been such,” replied Elizabeth, “as neither you, nor I, nor anybody can ever forget.
BEG_SENT It is useless to talk of it.” It now occurred to the girls that their mother was in all likelihood perfectly ignorant of what had happened.
BEG_SENT They went to the library, therefore, and asked their father whether he would not wish them to make it known to her.
BEG_SENT He was writing and, without raising his head, coolly replied: “Just as you please.” “May we take my uncle’s letter to read to her?” “Take whatever you like, and get away.” Elizabeth took the letter from his writing-table, and they went up stairs together.
BEG_SENT Mary and Kitty were both with Mrs. Bennet: one communication would, therefore, do for all.
BEG_SENT After a slight preparation for good news, the letter was read aloud.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet could hardly contain herself.
BEG_SENT As soon as Jane had read Mr. Gardiner’s hope of Lydia’s being soon married, her joy burst forth, and every following sentence added to its exuberance.
BEG_SENT She was now in an irritation as violent from delight, as she had ever been fidgety from alarm and vexation.
BEG_SENT To know that her daughter would be married was enough.
BEG_SENT She was disturbed by no fear for her felicity, nor humbled by any remembrance of her misconduct.
BEG_SENT “My dear, dear Lydia!” she cried.
BEG_SENT “This is delightful indeed!
BEG_SENT She will be married!
BEG_SENT I shall see her again!
BEG_SENT She will be married at sixteen!
BEG_SENT My good, kind brother!
BEG_SENT I knew how it would be.
BEG_SENT I knew he would manage everything!
BEG_SENT How I long to see her!
BEG_SENT and to see dear Wickham too!
BEG_SENT But the clothes, the wedding clothes!
BEG_SENT I will write to my sister Gardiner about them directly.
BEG_SENT Lizzy, my dear, run down to your father, and ask him how much he will give her.
BEG_SENT Stay, stay, I will go myself.
BEG_SENT Ring the bell, Kitty, for Hill.
BEG_SENT I will put on my things in a moment.
BEG_SENT My dear, dear Lydia!
BEG_SENT How merry we shall be together when we meet!” Her eldest daughter endeavoured to give some relief to the violence of these transports, by leading her thoughts to the obligations which Mr. Gardiner’s behaviour laid them all under.
BEG_SENT “For we must attribute this happy conclusion,” she added, “in a great measure to his kindness.
BEG_SENT We are persuaded that he has pledged himself to assist Mr. Wickham with money.” “Well,” cried her mother, “it is all very right; who should do it but her own uncle?
BEG_SENT If he had not had a family of his own, I and my children must have had all his money, you know; and it is the first time we have ever had anything from him, except a few presents.
BEG_SENT Well!
BEG_SENT I am so happy!
BEG_SENT In a short time I shall have a daughter married.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Wickham!
BEG_SENT How well it sounds!
BEG_SENT And she was only sixteen last June.
BEG_SENT My dear Jane, I am in such a flutter, that I am sure I can’t write; so I will dictate, and you write for me.
BEG_SENT We will settle with your father about the money afterwards; but the things should be ordered immediately.” She was then proceeding to all the particulars of calico, muslin, and cambric, and would shortly have dictated some very plentiful orders, had not Jane, though with some difficulty, persuaded her to wait till her father was at leisure to be consulted.
BEG_SENT One day’s delay, she observed, would be of small importance; and her mother was too happy to be quite so obstinate as usual.
BEG_SENT Other schemes, too, came into her head.
BEG_SENT “I will go to Meryton,” said she, “as soon as I am dressed, and tell the good, good news to my sister Philips.
BEG_SENT And as I come back, I can call on Lady Lucas and Mrs. Long.
BEG_SENT Kitty, run down and order the carriage.
BEG_SENT An airing would do me a great deal of good, I am sure.
BEG_SENT Girls, can I do anything for you in Meryton?
BEG_SENT Oh!
BEG_SENT Here comes Hill!
BEG_SENT My dear Hill, have you heard the good news?
BEG_SENT Miss Lydia is going to be married; and you shall all have a bowl of punch to make merry at her wedding.” Mrs. Hill began instantly to express her joy.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth received her congratulations amongst the rest, and then, sick of this folly, took refuge in her own room, that she might think with freedom.
BEG_SENT Poor Lydia’s situation must, at best, be bad enough; but that it was no worse, she had need to be thankful.
BEG_SENT She felt it so; and though, in looking forward, neither rational happiness nor worldly prosperity could be justly expected for her sister, in looking back to what they had feared, only two hours ago, she felt all the advantages of what they had gained.
BEG_SENT   Mr. Bennet had very often wished before this period of his life that, instead of spending his whole income, he had laid by an annual sum for the better provision of his children, and of his wife, if she survived him.
BEG_SENT He now wished it more than ever.
BEG_SENT Had he done his duty in that respect, Lydia need not have been indebted to her uncle for whatever of honour or credit could now be purchased for her.
BEG_SENT The satisfaction of prevailing on one of the most worthless young men in Great Britain to be her husband might then have rested in its proper place.
BEG_SENT He was seriously concerned that a cause of so little advantage to anyone should be forwarded at the sole expense of his brother-in-law, and he was determined, if possible, to find out the extent of his assistance, and to discharge the obligation as soon as he could.
BEG_SENT When first Mr. Bennet had married, economy was held to be perfectly useless, for, of course, they were to have a son.
BEG_SENT The son was to join in cutting off the entail, as soon as he should be of age, and the widow and younger children would by that means be provided for.
BEG_SENT Five daughters successively entered the world, but yet the son was to come; and Mrs. Bennet, for many years after Lydia’s birth, had been certain that he would.
BEG_SENT This event had at last been despaired of, but it was then too late to be saving.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet had no turn for economy, and her husband’s love of independence had alone prevented their exceeding their income.
BEG_SENT Five thousand pounds was settled by marriage articles on Mrs. Bennet and the children.
BEG_SENT But in what proportions it should be divided amongst the latter depended on the will of the parents.
BEG_SENT This was one point, with regard to Lydia, at least, which was now to be settled, and Mr. Bennet could have no hesitation in acceding to the proposal before him.
BEG_SENT In terms of grateful acknowledgment for the kindness of his brother, though expressed most concisely, he then delivered on paper his perfect approbation of all that was done, and his willingness to fulfil the engagements that had been made for him.
BEG_SENT He had never before supposed that, could Wickham be prevailed on to marry his daughter, it would be done with so little inconvenience to himself as by the present arrangement.
BEG_SENT He would scarcely be ten pounds a year the loser by the hundred that was to be paid them; for, what with her board and pocket allowance, and the continual presents in money which passed to her through her mother’s hands, Lydia’s expenses had been very little within that sum.
BEG_SENT That it would be done with such trifling exertion on his side, too, was another very welcome surprise; for his wish at present was to have as little trouble in the business as possible.
BEG_SENT When the first transports of rage which had produced his activity in seeking her were over, he naturally returned to all his former indolence.
BEG_SENT His letter was soon dispatched; for, though dilatory in undertaking business, he was quick in its execution.
BEG_SENT He begged to know further particulars of what he was indebted to his brother, but was too angry with Lydia to send any message to her.
BEG_SENT The good news spread quickly through the house, and with proportionate speed through the neighbourhood.
BEG_SENT It was borne in the latter with decent philosophy.
BEG_SENT To be sure, it would have been more for the advantage of conversation had Miss Lydia Bennet come upon the town; or, as the happiest alternative, been secluded from the world, in some distant farmhouse.
BEG_SENT But there was much to be talked of in marrying her; and the good-natured wishes for her well-doing which had proceeded before from all the spiteful old ladies in Meryton lost but a little of their spirit in this change of circumstances, because with such an husband her misery was considered certain.
BEG_SENT It was a fortnight since Mrs. Bennet had been downstairs; but on this happy day she again took her seat at the head of her table, and in spirits oppressively high.
BEG_SENT No sentiment of shame gave a damp to her triumph.
BEG_SENT The marriage of a daughter, which had been the first object of her wishes since Jane was sixteen, was now on the point of accomplishment, and her thoughts and her words ran wholly on those attendants of elegant nuptials, fine muslins, new carriages, and servants.
BEG_SENT She was busily searching through the neighbourhood for a proper situation for her daughter, and, without knowing or considering what their income might be, rejected many as deficient in size and importance.
BEG_SENT “Haye Park might do,” said she, “if the Gouldings could quit it—or the great house at Stoke, if the drawing-room were larger; but Ashworth is too far off!
BEG_SENT I could not bear to have her ten miles from me; and as for Pulvis Lodge, the attics are dreadful.” Her husband allowed her to talk on without interruption while the servants remained.
BEG_SENT But when they had withdrawn, he said to her: “Mrs.
BEG_SENT Bennet, before you take any or all of these houses for your son and daughter, let us come to a right understanding.
BEG_SENT Into _one_ house in this neighbourhood they shall never have admittance.
BEG_SENT I will not encourage the impudence of either, by receiving them at Longbourn.” A long dispute followed this declaration; but Mr. Bennet was firm.
BEG_SENT It soon led to another; and Mrs. Bennet found, with amazement and horror, that her husband would not advance a guinea to buy clothes for his daughter.
BEG_SENT He protested that she should receive from him no mark of affection whatever on the occasion.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet could hardly comprehend it.
BEG_SENT That his anger could be carried to such a point of inconceivable resentment as to refuse his daughter a privilege without which her marriage would scarcely seem valid, exceeded all she could believe possible.
BEG_SENT She was more alive to the disgrace which her want of new clothes must reflect on her daughter’s nuptials, than to any sense of shame at her eloping and living with Wickham a fortnight before they took place.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth was now most heartily sorry that she had, from the distress of the moment, been led to make Mr. Darcy acquainted with their fears for her sister; for since her marriage would so shortly give the proper termination to the elopement, they might hope to conceal its unfavourable beginning from all those who were not immediately on the spot.
BEG_SENT She had no fear of its spreading farther through his means.
BEG_SENT There were few people on whose secrecy she would have more confidently depended; but, at the same time, there was no one whose knowledge of a sister’s frailty would have mortified her so much—not, however, from any fear of disadvantage from it individually to herself, for, at any rate, there seemed a gulf impassable between them.
BEG_SENT Had Lydia’s marriage been concluded on the most honourable terms, it was not to be supposed that Mr. Darcy would connect himself with a family where, to every other objection, would now be added an alliance and relationship of the nearest kind with a man whom he so justly scorned.
BEG_SENT From such a connection she could not wonder that he would shrink.
BEG_SENT The wish of procuring her regard, which she had assured herself of his feeling in Derbyshire, could not in rational expectation survive such a blow as this.
BEG_SENT She was humbled, she was grieved; she repented, though she hardly knew of what.
BEG_SENT She became jealous of his esteem, when she could no longer hope to be benefited by it.
BEG_SENT She wanted to hear of him, when there seemed the least chance of gaining intelligence.
BEG_SENT She was convinced that she could have been happy with him, when it was no longer likely they should meet.
BEG_SENT What a triumph for him, as she often thought, could he know that the proposals which she had proudly spurned only four months ago, would now have been most gladly and gratefully received!
BEG_SENT He was as generous, she doubted not, as the most generous of his sex; but while he was mortal, there must be a triumph.
BEG_SENT She began now to comprehend that he was exactly the man who, in disposition and talents, would most suit her.
BEG_SENT His understanding and temper, though unlike her own, would have answered all her wishes.
BEG_SENT It was an union that must have been to the advantage of both; by her ease and liveliness, his mind might have been softened, his manners improved; and from his judgement, information, and knowledge of the world, she must have received benefit of greater importance.
BEG_SENT But no such happy marriage could now teach the admiring multitude what connubial felicity really was.
BEG_SENT An union of a different tendency, and precluding the possibility of the other, was soon to be formed in their family.
BEG_SENT How Wickham and Lydia were to be supported in tolerable independence, she could not imagine.
BEG_SENT But how little of permanent happiness could belong to a couple who were only brought together because their passions were stronger than their virtue, she could easily conjecture.
BEG_SENT Mr. Gardiner soon wrote again to his brother.
BEG_SENT To Mr. Bennet’s acknowledgments he briefly replied, with assurance of his eagerness to promote the welfare of any of his family; and concluded with entreaties that the subject might never be mentioned to him again.
BEG_SENT The principal purport of his letter was to inform them that Mr. Wickham had resolved on quitting the militia.
BEG_SENT “It was greatly my wish that he should do so,” he added, “as soon as his marriage was fixed on.
BEG_SENT And I think you will agree with me, in considering the removal from that corps as highly advisable, both on his account and my niece’s.
BEG_SENT It is Mr. Wickham’s intention to go into the regulars; and among his former friends, there are still some who are able and willing to assist him in the army.
BEG_SENT He has the promise of an ensigncy in General ——’s regiment, now quartered in the North.
BEG_SENT It is an advantage to have it so far from this part of the kingdom.
BEG_SENT He promises fairly; and I hope among different people, where they may each have a character to preserve, they will both be more prudent.
BEG_SENT I have written to Colonel Forster, to inform him of our present arrangements, and to request that he will satisfy the various creditors of Mr. Wickham in and near Brighton, with assurances of speedy payment, for which I have pledged myself.
BEG_SENT And will you give yourself the trouble of carrying similar assurances to his creditors in Meryton, of whom I shall subjoin a list according to his information?
BEG_SENT He has given in all his debts; I hope at least he has not deceived us.
BEG_SENT Haggerston has our directions, and all will be completed in a week.
BEG_SENT They will then join his regiment, unless they are first invited to Longbourn; and I understand from Mrs. Gardiner, that my niece is very desirous of seeing you all before she leaves the South.
BEG_SENT She is well, and begs to be dutifully remembered to you and her mother.—Yours, etc., “E.
BEG_SENT GARDINER.” Mr. Bennet and his daughters saw all the advantages of Wickham’s removal from the ——shire as clearly as Mr. Gardiner could do.
BEG_SENT But Mrs. Bennet was not so well pleased with it.
BEG_SENT Lydia’s being settled in the North, just when she had expected most pleasure and pride in her company, for she had by no means given up her plan of their residing in Hertfordshire, was a severe disappointment; and, besides, it was such a pity that Lydia should be taken from a regiment where she was acquainted with everybody, and had so many favourites.
BEG_SENT “She is so fond of Mrs. Forster,” said she, “it will be quite shocking to send her away!
BEG_SENT And there are several of the young men, too, that she likes very much.
BEG_SENT The officers may not be so pleasant in General ——’s regiment.” His daughter’s request, for such it might be considered, of being admitted into her family again before she set off for the North, received at first an absolute negative.
BEG_SENT But Jane and Elizabeth, who agreed in wishing, for the sake of their sister’s feelings and consequence, that she should be noticed on her marriage by her parents, urged him so earnestly yet so rationally and so mildly, to receive her and her husband at Longbourn, as soon as they were married, that he was prevailed on to think as they thought, and act as they wished.
BEG_SENT And their mother had the satisfaction of knowing that she would be able to show her married daughter in the neighbourhood before she was banished to the North.
BEG_SENT When Mr. Bennet wrote again to his brother, therefore, he sent his permission for them to come; and it was settled, that as soon as the ceremony was over, they should proceed to Longbourn.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth was surprised, however, that Wickham should consent to such a scheme, and had she consulted only her own inclination, any meeting with him would have been the last object of her wishes.
BEG_SENT   Their sister’s wedding day arrived; and Jane and Elizabeth felt for her probably more than she felt for herself.
BEG_SENT The carriage was sent to meet them at ——, and they were to return in it by dinner-time.
BEG_SENT Their arrival was dreaded by the elder Miss Bennets, and Jane more especially, who gave Lydia the feelings which would have attended herself, had _she_ been the culprit, and was wretched in the thought of what her sister must endure.
BEG_SENT They came.
BEG_SENT The family were assembled in the breakfast room to receive them.
BEG_SENT Smiles decked the face of Mrs. Bennet as the carriage drove up to the door; her husband looked impenetrably grave; her daughters, alarmed, anxious, uneasy.
BEG_SENT Lydia’s voice was heard in the vestibule; the door was thrown open, and she ran into the room.
BEG_SENT Her mother stepped forwards, embraced her, and welcomed her with rapture; gave her hand, with an affectionate smile, to Wickham, who followed his lady; and wished them both joy with an alacrity which shewed no doubt of their happiness.
BEG_SENT Their reception from Mr. Bennet, to whom they then turned, was not quite so cordial.
BEG_SENT His countenance rather gained in austerity; and he scarcely opened his lips.
BEG_SENT The easy assurance of the young couple, indeed, was enough to provoke him.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth was disgusted, and even Miss Bennet was shocked.
BEG_SENT Lydia was Lydia still; untamed, unabashed, wild, noisy, and fearless.
BEG_SENT She turned from sister to sister, demanding their congratulations; and when at length they all sat down, looked eagerly round the room, took notice of some little alteration in it, and observed, with a laugh, that it was a great while since she had been there.
BEG_SENT Wickham was not at all more distressed than herself, but his manners were always so pleasing, that had his character and his marriage been exactly what they ought, his smiles and his easy address, while he claimed their relationship, would have delighted them all.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth had not before believed him quite equal to such assurance; but she sat down, resolving within herself to draw no limits in future to the impudence of an impudent man.
BEG_SENT _She_ blushed, and Jane blushed; but the cheeks of the two who caused their confusion suffered no variation of colour.
BEG_SENT There was no want of discourse.
BEG_SENT The bride and her mother could neither of them talk fast enough; and Wickham, who happened to sit near Elizabeth, began inquiring after his acquaintance in that neighbourhood, with a good humoured ease which she felt very unable to equal in her replies.
BEG_SENT They seemed each of them to have the happiest memories in the world.
BEG_SENT Nothing of the past was recollected with pain; and Lydia led voluntarily to subjects which her sisters would not have alluded to for the world.
BEG_SENT “Only think of its being three months,” she cried, “since I went away; it seems but a fortnight I declare; and yet there have been things enough happened in the time.
BEG_SENT Good gracious!
BEG_SENT when I went away, I am sure I had no more idea of being married till I came back again!
BEG_SENT though I thought it would be very good fun if I was.” Her father lifted up his eyes.
BEG_SENT Jane was distressed.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth looked expressively at Lydia; but she, who never heard nor saw anything of which she chose to be insensible, gaily continued, “Oh!
BEG_SENT mamma, do the people hereabouts know I am married to-day?
BEG_SENT I was afraid they might not; and we overtook William Goulding in his curricle, so I was determined he should know it, and so I let down the side-glass next to him, and took off my glove, and let my hand just rest upon the window frame, so that he might see the ring, and then I bowed and smiled like anything.” Elizabeth could bear it no longer.
BEG_SENT She got up, and ran out of the room; and returned no more, till she heard them passing through the hall to the dining parlour.
BEG_SENT She then joined them soon enough to see Lydia, with anxious parade, walk up to her mother’s right hand, and hear her say to her eldest sister, “Ah!
BEG_SENT Jane, I take your place now, and you must go lower, because I am a married woman.” It was not to be supposed that time would give Lydia that embarrassment from which she had been so wholly free at first.
BEG_SENT Her ease and good spirits increased.
BEG_SENT She longed to see Mrs. Phillips, the Lucases, and all their other neighbours, and to hear herself called “Mrs.
BEG_SENT Wickham” by each of them; and in the mean time, she went after dinner to show her ring, and boast of being married, to Mrs. Hill and the two housemaids.
BEG_SENT “Well, mamma,” said she, when they were all returned to the breakfast room, “and what do you think of my husband?
BEG_SENT Is not he a charming man?
BEG_SENT I am sure my sisters must all envy me.
BEG_SENT I only hope they may have half my good luck.
BEG_SENT They must all go to Brighton.
BEG_SENT That is the place to get husbands.
BEG_SENT What a pity it is, mamma, we did not all go.” “Very true; and if I had my will, we should.
BEG_SENT But my dear Lydia, I don’t at all like your going such a way off.
BEG_SENT Must it be so?” “Oh, lord!
BEG_SENT yes;—there is nothing in that.
BEG_SENT I shall like it of all things.
BEG_SENT You and papa, and my sisters, must come down and see us.
BEG_SENT We shall be at Newcastle all the winter, and I dare say there will be some balls, and I will take care to get good partners for them all.” “I should like it beyond anything!” said her mother.
BEG_SENT “And then when you go away, you may leave one or two of my sisters behind you; and I dare say I shall get husbands for them before the winter is over.” “I thank you for my share of the favour,” said Elizabeth; “but I do not particularly like your way of getting husbands.” Their visitors were not to remain above ten days with them.
BEG_SENT Mr. Wickham had received his commission before he left London, and he was to join his regiment at the end of a fortnight.
BEG_SENT No one but Mrs. Bennet regretted that their stay would be so short; and she made the most of the time by visiting about with her daughter, and having very frequent parties at home.
BEG_SENT These parties were acceptable to all; to avoid a family circle was even more desirable to such as did think, than such as did not.
BEG_SENT Wickham’s affection for Lydia was just what Elizabeth had expected to find it; not equal to Lydia’s for him.
BEG_SENT She had scarcely needed her present observation to be satisfied, from the reason of things, that their elopement had been brought on by the strength of her love, rather than by his; and she would have wondered why, without violently caring for her, he chose to elope with her at all, had she not felt certain that his flight was rendered necessary by distress of circumstances; and if that were the case, he was not the young man to resist an opportunity of having a companion.
BEG_SENT Lydia was exceedingly fond of him.
BEG_SENT He was her dear Wickham on every occasion; no one was to be put in competition with him.
BEG_SENT He did every thing best in the world; and she was sure he would kill more birds on the first of September, than any body else in the country.
BEG_SENT One morning, soon after their arrival, as she was sitting with her two elder sisters, she said to Elizabeth: “Lizzy, I never gave _you_ an account of my wedding, I believe.
BEG_SENT You were not by, when I told mamma and the others all about it.
BEG_SENT Are not you curious to hear how it was managed?” “No really,” replied Elizabeth; “I think there cannot be too little said on the subject.” “La!
BEG_SENT You are so strange!
BEG_SENT But I must tell you how it went off.
BEG_SENT We were married, you know, at St. Clement’s, because Wickham’s lodgings were in that parish.
BEG_SENT And it was settled that we should all be there by eleven o’clock.
BEG_SENT My uncle and aunt and I were to go together; and the others were to meet us at the church.
BEG_SENT Well, Monday morning came, and I was in such a fuss!
BEG_SENT I was so afraid, you know, that something would happen to put it off, and then I should have gone quite distracted.
BEG_SENT And there was my aunt, all the time I was dressing, preaching and talking away just as if she was reading a sermon.
BEG_SENT However, I did not hear above one word in ten, for I was thinking, you may suppose, of my dear Wickham.
BEG_SENT I longed to know whether he would be married in his blue coat.” “Well, and so we breakfasted at ten as usual; I thought it would never be over; for, by the bye, you are to understand, that my uncle and aunt were horrid unpleasant all the time I was with them.
BEG_SENT If you’ll believe me, I did not once put my foot out of doors, though I was there a fortnight.
BEG_SENT Not one party, or scheme, or anything.
BEG_SENT To be sure London was rather thin, but, however, the Little Theatre was open.
BEG_SENT Well, and so just as the carriage came to the door, my uncle was called away upon business to that horrid man Mr. Stone.
BEG_SENT And then, you know, when once they get together, there is no end of it.
BEG_SENT Well, I was so frightened I did not know what to do, for my uncle was to give me away; and if we were beyond the hour, we could not be married all day.
BEG_SENT But, luckily, he came back again in ten minutes’ time, and then we all set out.
BEG_SENT However, I recollected afterwards that if he _had_ been prevented going, the wedding need not be put off, for Mr. Darcy might have done as well.” “Mr.
BEG_SENT Darcy!” repeated Elizabeth, in utter amazement.
BEG_SENT “Oh, yes!—he was to come there with Wickham, you know.
BEG_SENT But gracious me!
BEG_SENT I quite forgot!
BEG_SENT I ought not to have said a word about it.
BEG_SENT I promised them so faithfully!
BEG_SENT What will Wickham say?
BEG_SENT It was to be such a secret!” “If it was to be secret,” said Jane, “say not another word on the subject.
BEG_SENT You may depend upon my seeking no further.” “Oh!
BEG_SENT certainly,” said Elizabeth, though burning with curiosity; “we will ask you no questions.” “Thank you,” said Lydia, “for if you did, I should certainly tell you all, and then Wickham would be angry.” On such encouragement to ask, Elizabeth was forced to put it out of her power, by running away.
BEG_SENT But to live in ignorance on such a point was impossible; or at least it was impossible not to try for information.
BEG_SENT Mr. Darcy had been at her sister’s wedding.
BEG_SENT It was exactly a scene, and exactly among people, where he had apparently least to do, and least temptation to go.
BEG_SENT Conjectures as to the meaning of it, rapid and wild, hurried into her brain; but she was satisfied with none.
BEG_SENT Those that best pleased her, as placing his conduct in the noblest light, seemed most improbable.
BEG_SENT She could not bear such suspense; and hastily seizing a sheet of paper, wrote a short letter to her aunt, to request an explanation of what Lydia had dropt, if it were compatible with the secrecy which had been intended.
BEG_SENT “You may readily comprehend,” she added, “what my curiosity must be to know how a person unconnected with any of us, and (comparatively speaking) a stranger to our family, should have been amongst you at such a time.
BEG_SENT Pray write instantly, and let me understand it—unless it is, for very cogent reasons, to remain in the secrecy which Lydia seems to think necessary; and then I must endeavour to be satisfied with ignorance.” “Not that I _shall_, though,” she added to herself, as she finished the letter; “and my dear aunt, if you do not tell me in an honourable manner, I shall certainly be reduced to tricks and stratagems to find it out.” Jane’s delicate sense of honour would not allow her to speak to Elizabeth privately of what Lydia had let fall; Elizabeth was glad of it;—till it appeared whether her inquiries would receive any satisfaction, she had rather be without a confidante.
BEG_SENT   Elizabeth had the satisfaction of receiving an answer to her letter as soon as she possibly could.
BEG_SENT She was no sooner in possession of it than, hurrying into the little copse, where she was least likely to be interrupted, she sat down on one of the benches and prepared to be happy; for the length of the letter convinced her that it did not contain a denial.
BEG_SENT “Gracechurch Street, _Sept_.
BEG_SENT .
BEG_SENT “My dear Niece, “I have just received your letter, and shall devote this whole morning to answering it, as I foresee that a _little_ writing will not comprise what I have to tell you.
BEG_SENT I must confess myself surprised by your application; I did not expect it from _you_.
BEG_SENT Don’t think me angry, however, for I only mean to let you know that I had not imagined such inquiries to be necessary on _your_ side.
BEG_SENT If you do not choose to understand me, forgive my impertinence.
BEG_SENT Your uncle is as much surprised as I am—and nothing but the belief of your being a party concerned would have allowed him to act as he has done.
BEG_SENT But if you are really innocent and ignorant, I must be more explicit.
BEG_SENT “On the very day of my coming home from Longbourn, your uncle had a most unexpected visitor.
BEG_SENT Mr. Darcy called, and was shut up with him several hours.
BEG_SENT It was all over before I arrived; so my curiosity was not so dreadfully racked as _yours_ seems to have been.
BEG_SENT He came to tell Mr. Gardiner that he had found out where your sister and Mr. Wickham were, and that he had seen and talked with them both; Wickham repeatedly, Lydia once.
BEG_SENT From what I can collect, he left Derbyshire only one day after ourselves, and came to town with the resolution of hunting for them.
BEG_SENT The motive professed was his conviction of its being owing to himself that Wickham’s worthlessness had not been so well known as to make it impossible for any young woman of character to love or confide in him.
BEG_SENT He generously imputed the whole to his mistaken pride, and confessed that he had before thought it beneath him to lay his private actions open to the world.
BEG_SENT His character was to speak for itself.
BEG_SENT He called it, therefore, his duty to step forward, and endeavour to remedy an evil which had been brought on by himself.
BEG_SENT If he _had another_ motive, I am sure it would never disgrace him.
BEG_SENT He had been some days in town, before he was able to discover them; but he had something to direct his search, which was more than _we_ had; and the consciousness of this was another reason for his resolving to follow us.
BEG_SENT “There is a lady, it seems, a Mrs. Younge, who was some time ago governess to Miss Darcy, and was dismissed from her charge on some cause of disapprobation, though he did not say what.
BEG_SENT She then took a large house in Edward-street, and has since maintained herself by letting lodgings.
BEG_SENT This Mrs. Younge was, he knew, intimately acquainted with Wickham; and he went to her for intelligence of him as soon as he got to town.
BEG_SENT But it was two or three days before he could get from her what he wanted.
BEG_SENT She would not betray her trust, I suppose, without bribery and corruption, for she really did know where her friend was to be found.
BEG_SENT Wickham indeed had gone to her on their first arrival in London, and had she been able to receive them into her house, they would have taken up their abode with her.
BEG_SENT At length, however, our kind friend procured the wished-for direction.
BEG_SENT They were in —— street.
BEG_SENT He saw Wickham, and afterwards insisted on seeing Lydia.
BEG_SENT His first object with her, he acknowledged, had been to persuade her to quit her present disgraceful situation, and return to her friends as soon as they could be prevailed on to receive her, offering his assistance, as far as it would go.
BEG_SENT But he found Lydia absolutely resolved on remaining where she was.
BEG_SENT She cared for none of her friends; she wanted no help of his; she would not hear of leaving Wickham.
BEG_SENT She was sure they should be married some time or other, and it did not much signify when.
BEG_SENT Since such were her feelings, it only remained, he thought, to secure and expedite a marriage, which, in his very first conversation with Wickham, he easily learnt had never been _his_ design.
BEG_SENT He confessed himself obliged to leave the regiment, on account of some debts of honour, which were very pressing; and scrupled not to lay all the ill-consequences of Lydia’s flight on her own folly alone.
BEG_SENT He meant to resign his commission immediately; and as to his future situation, he could conjecture very little about it.
BEG_SENT He must go somewhere, but he did not know where, and he knew he should have nothing to live on.
BEG_SENT “Mr.
BEG_SENT Darcy asked him why he had not married your sister at once.
BEG_SENT Though Mr. Bennet was not imagined to be very rich, he would have been able to do something for him, and his situation must have been benefited by marriage.
BEG_SENT But he found, in reply to this question, that Wickham still cherished the hope of more effectually making his fortune by marriage in some other country.
BEG_SENT Under such circumstances, however, he was not likely to be proof against the temptation of immediate relief.
BEG_SENT “They met several times, for there was much to be discussed.
BEG_SENT Wickham of course wanted more than he could get; but at length was reduced to be reasonable.
BEG_SENT “Everything being settled between _them_, Mr. Darcy’s next step was to make your uncle acquainted with it, and he first called in Gracechurch street the evening before I came home.
BEG_SENT But Mr. Gardiner could not be seen, and Mr. Darcy found, on further inquiry, that your father was still with him, but would quit town the next morning.
BEG_SENT He did not judge your father to be a person whom he could so properly consult as your uncle, and therefore readily postponed seeing him till after the departure of the former.
BEG_SENT He did not leave his name, and till the next day it was only known that a gentleman had called on business.
BEG_SENT “On Saturday he came again.
BEG_SENT Your father was gone, your uncle at home, and, as I said before, they had a great deal of talk together.
BEG_SENT “They met again on Sunday, and then _I_ saw him too.
BEG_SENT It was not all settled before Monday: as soon as it was, the express was sent off to Longbourn.
BEG_SENT But our visitor was very obstinate.
BEG_SENT I fancy, Lizzy, that obstinacy is the real defect of his character, after all.
BEG_SENT He has been accused of many faults at different times, but _this_ is the true one.
BEG_SENT Nothing was to be done that he did not do himself; though I am sure (and I do not speak it to be thanked, therefore say nothing about it), your uncle would most readily have settled the whole.
BEG_SENT “They battled it together for a long time, which was more than either the gentleman or lady concerned in it deserved.
BEG_SENT But at last your uncle was forced to yield, and instead of being allowed to be of use to his niece, was forced to put up with only having the probable credit of it, which went sorely against the grain; and I really believe your letter this morning gave him great pleasure, because it required an explanation that would rob him of his borrowed feathers, and give the praise where it was due.
BEG_SENT But, Lizzy, this must go no farther than yourself, or Jane at most.
BEG_SENT “You know pretty well, I suppose, what has been done for the young people.
BEG_SENT His debts are to be paid, amounting, I believe, to considerably more than a thousand pounds, another thousand in addition to her own settled upon _her_, and his commission purchased.
BEG_SENT The reason why all this was to be done by him alone, was such as I have given above.
BEG_SENT It was owing to him, to his reserve and want of proper consideration, that Wickham’s character had been so misunderstood, and consequently that he had been received and noticed as he was.
BEG_SENT Perhaps there was some truth in _this_; though I doubt whether _his_ reserve, or _anybody’s_ reserve, can be answerable for the event.
BEG_SENT But in spite of all this fine talking, my dear Lizzy, you may rest perfectly assured that your uncle would never have yielded, if we had not given him credit for _another interest_ in the affair.
BEG_SENT “When all this was resolved on, he returned again to his friends, who were still staying at Pemberley; but it was agreed that he should be in London once more when the wedding took place, and all money matters were then to receive the last finish.
BEG_SENT “I believe I have now told you every thing.
BEG_SENT It is a relation which you tell me is to give you great surprise; I hope at least it will not afford you any displeasure.
BEG_SENT Lydia came to us; and Wickham had constant admission to the house.
BEG_SENT _He_ was exactly what he had been, when I knew him in Hertfordshire; but I would not tell you how little I was satisfied with _her_ behaviour while she staid with us, if I had not perceived, by Jane’s letter last Wednesday, that her conduct on coming home was exactly of a piece with it, and therefore what I now tell you can give you no fresh pain.
BEG_SENT I talked to her repeatedly in the most serious manner, representing to her all the wickedness of what she had done, and all the unhappiness she had brought on her family.
BEG_SENT If she heard me, it was by good luck, for I am sure she did not listen.
BEG_SENT I was sometimes quite provoked, but then I recollected my dear Elizabeth and Jane, and for their sakes had patience with her.
BEG_SENT “Mr.
BEG_SENT Darcy was punctual in his return, and as Lydia informed you, attended the wedding.
BEG_SENT He dined with us the next day, and was to leave town again on Wednesday or Thursday.

BEG_SENT His behaviour to us has, in every respect, been as pleasing as when we were in Derbyshire.
BEG_SENT His understanding and opinions all please me; he wants nothing but a little more liveliness, and _that_, if he marry _prudently_, his wife may teach him.

BEG_SENT “But I must write no more.
BEG_SENT The children have been wanting me this half hour.
BEG_SENT “Yours, very sincerely, “M.
BEG_SENT GARDINER.” The contents of this letter threw Elizabeth into a flutter of spirits, in which it was difficult to determine whether pleasure or pain bore the greatest share.
BEG_SENT The vague and unsettled suspicions which uncertainty had produced of what Mr. Darcy might have been doing to forward her sister’s match, which she had feared to encourage as an exertion of goodness too great to be probable, and at the same time dreaded to be just, from the pain of obligation, were proved beyond their greatest extent to be true!
BEG_SENT He had followed them purposely to town, he had taken on himself all the trouble and mortification attendant on such a research; in which supplication had been necessary to a woman whom he must abominate and despise, and where he was reduced to meet, frequently meet, reason with, persuade, and finally bribe, the man whom he always most wished to avoid, and whose very name it was punishment to him to pronounce.
BEG_SENT He had done all this for a girl whom he could neither regard nor esteem.
BEG_SENT Her heart did whisper that he had done it for her.
BEG_SENT But it was a hope shortly checked by other considerations, and she soon felt that even her vanity was insufficient, when required to depend on his affection for her—for a woman who had already refused him—as able to overcome a sentiment so natural as abhorrence against relationship with Wickham.
BEG_SENT Brother-in-law of Wickham!
BEG_SENT Every kind of pride must revolt from the connection.
BEG_SENT He had, to be sure, done much.
BEG_SENT She was ashamed to think how much.
BEG_SENT But he had given a reason for his interference, which asked no extraordinary stretch of belief.
BEG_SENT It was reasonable that he should feel he had been wrong; he had liberality, and he had the means of exercising it; and though she would not place herself as his principal inducement, she could, perhaps, believe that remaining partiality for her might assist his endeavours in a cause where her peace of mind must be materially concerned.
BEG_SENT It was painful, exceedingly painful, to know that they were under obligations to a person who could never receive a return.
BEG_SENT They owed the restoration of Lydia, her character, every thing, to him.
BEG_SENT Oh!
BEG_SENT how heartily did she grieve over every ungracious sensation she had ever encouraged, every saucy speech she had ever directed towards him.
BEG_SENT For herself she was humbled; but she was proud of him.
BEG_SENT Proud that in a cause of compassion and honour, he had been able to get the better of himself.

BEG_SENT It was hardly enough; but it pleased her.
BEG_SENT She was even sensible of some pleasure, though mixed with regret, on finding how steadfastly both she and her uncle had been persuaded that affection and confidence subsisted between Mr. Darcy and herself.
BEG_SENT She was roused from her seat, and her reflections, by some one’s approach; and before she could strike into another path, she was overtaken by Wickham.
BEG_SENT “I am afraid I interrupt your solitary ramble, my dear sister?” said he, as he joined her.
BEG_SENT “You certainly do,” she replied with a smile; “but it does not follow that the interruption must be unwelcome.” “I should be sorry indeed, if it were.
BEG_SENT _We_ were always good friends; and now we are better.” “True.
BEG_SENT Are the others coming out?” “I do not know.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet and Lydia are going in the carriage to Meryton.

BEG_SENT “I almost envy you the pleasure, and yet I believe it would be too much for me, or else I could take it in my way to Newcastle.

BEG_SENT I should have considered it as part of my duty, and the exertion would soon have been nothing.

BEG_SENT and it was not wholly without foundation.
BEG_SENT You may remember what I told you on that point, when first we talked of it.” They were now almost at the door of the house, for she had walked fast to get rid of him; and unwilling, for her sister’s sake, to provoke him, she only said in reply, with a good-humoured smile: “Come, Mr. Wickham, we are brother and sister, you know.
BEG_SENT Do not let us quarrel about the past.
BEG_SENT In future, I hope we shall be always of one mind.” She held out her hand; he kissed it with affectionate gallantry, though he hardly knew how to look, and they entered the house.
BEG_SENT   Mr. Wickham was so perfectly satisfied with this conversation that he never again distressed himself, or provoked his dear sister Elizabeth, by introducing the subject of it; and she was pleased to find that she had said enough to keep him quiet.
BEG_SENT The day of his and Lydia’s departure soon came, and Mrs. Bennet was forced to submit to a separation, which, as her husband by no means entered into her scheme of their all going to Newcastle, was likely to continue at least a twelvemonth.
BEG_SENT “Oh!

BEG_SENT I don’t know.
BEG_SENT Not these two or three years, perhaps.” “Write to me very often, my dear.” “As often as I can.
BEG_SENT But you know married women have never much time for writing.
BEG_SENT My sisters may write to _me_.
BEG_SENT They will have nothing else to do.” Mr. Wickham’s adieus were much more affectionate than his wife’s.
BEG_SENT He smiled, looked handsome, and said many pretty things.

BEG_SENT He simpers, and smirks, and makes love to us all.
BEG_SENT I am prodigiously proud of him.
BEG_SENT I defy even Sir William Lucas himself to produce a more valuable son-in-law.” The loss of her daughter made Mrs. Bennet very dull for several days.

BEG_SENT Lydia does not leave me because she is married, but only because her husband’s regiment happens to be so far off.
BEG_SENT If that had been nearer, she would not have gone so soon.” But the spiritless condition which this event threw her into was shortly relieved, and her mind opened again to the agitation of hope, by an article of news which then began to be in circulation.
BEG_SENT The housekeeper at Netherfield had received orders to prepare for the arrival of her master, who was coming down in a day or two, to shoot there for several weeks.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet was quite in the fidgets.
BEG_SENT She looked at Jane, and smiled and shook her head by turns.

BEG_SENT But, however, he is very welcome to come to Netherfield, if he likes it.
BEG_SENT And who knows what _may_ happen?
BEG_SENT But that is nothing to us.
BEG_SENT You know, sister, we agreed long ago never to mention a word about it.
BEG_SENT And so, is it quite certain he is coming?” “You may depend on it,” replied the other, “for Mrs. Nicholls was in Meryton last night; I saw her passing by, and went out myself on purpose to know the truth of it; and she told me that it was certain true.
BEG_SENT He comes down on Thursday at the latest, very likely on Wednesday.
BEG_SENT She was going to the butcher’s, she told me, on purpose to order in some meat on Wednesday, and she has got three couple of ducks just fit to be killed.” Miss Bennet had not been able to hear of his coming without changing colour.
BEG_SENT It was many months since she had mentioned his name to Elizabeth; but now, as soon as they were alone together, she said: “I saw you look at me to-day, Lizzy, when my aunt told us of the present report; and I know I appeared distressed.
BEG_SENT But don’t imagine it was from any silly cause.
BEG_SENT I was only confused for the moment, because I felt that I _should_ be looked at.

BEG_SENT I am glad of one thing, that he comes alone; because we shall see the less of him.

BEG_SENT Had she not seen him in Derbyshire, she might have supposed him capable of coming there with no other view than what was acknowledged; but she still thought him partial to Jane, and she wavered as to the greater probability of his coming there _with_ his friend’s permission, or being bold enough to come without it.
BEG_SENT “Yet it is hard,” she sometimes thought, “that this poor man cannot come to a house which he has legally hired, without raising all this speculation!

BEG_SENT They were more disturbed, more unequal, than she had often seen them.
BEG_SENT The subject which had been so warmly canvassed between their parents, about a twelvemonth ago, was now brought forward again.
BEG_SENT “As soon as ever Mr. Bingley comes, my dear,” said Mrs. Bennet, “you will wait on him of course.” “No, no.
BEG_SENT You forced me into visiting him last year, and promised, if I went to see him, he should marry one of my daughters.
BEG_SENT But it ended in nothing, and I will not be sent on a fool’s errand again.” His wife represented to him how absolutely necessary such an attention would be from all the neighbouring gentlemen, on his returning to Netherfield.
BEG_SENT “’Tis an _etiquette_ I despise,” said he.
BEG_SENT “If he wants our society, let him seek it.
BEG_SENT He knows where we live.
BEG_SENT I will not spend _my_ hours in running after my neighbours every time they go away and come back again.” “Well, all I know is, that it will be abominably rude if you do not wait on him.
BEG_SENT But, however, that shan’t prevent my asking him to dine here, I am determined.
BEG_SENT We must have Mrs. Long and the Gouldings soon.
BEG_SENT That will make thirteen with ourselves, so there will be just room at table for him.” Consoled by this resolution, she was the better able to bear her husband’s incivility; though it was very mortifying to know that her neighbours might all see Mr. Bingley, in consequence of it, before _they_ did.

BEG_SENT “It would be nothing; I could see him with perfect indifference, but I can hardly bear to hear it thus perpetually talked of.
BEG_SENT My mother means well; but she does not know, no one can know, how much I suffer from what she says.

BEG_SENT You must feel it; and the usual satisfaction of preaching patience to a sufferer is denied me, because you have always so much.” Mr. Bingley arrived.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet, through the assistance of servants, contrived to have the earliest tidings of it, that the period of anxiety and fretfulness on her side might be as long as it could.
BEG_SENT She counted the days that must intervene before their invitation could be sent; hopeless of seeing him before.
BEG_SENT But on the third morning after his arrival in Hertfordshire, she saw him, from her dressing-room window, enter the paddock and ride towards the house.
BEG_SENT Her daughters were eagerly called to partake of her joy.
BEG_SENT Jane resolutely kept her place at the table; but Elizabeth, to satisfy her mother, went to the window—she looked,—she saw Mr. Darcy with him, and sat down again by her sister.
BEG_SENT “There is a gentleman with him, mamma,” said Kitty; “who can it be?” “Some acquaintance or other, my dear, I suppose; I am sure I do not know.” “La!” replied Kitty, “it looks just like that man that used to be with him before.
BEG_SENT Mr. what’s-his-name.
BEG_SENT That tall, proud man.” “Good gracious!
BEG_SENT Mr. Darcy!—and so it does, I vow.
BEG_SENT Well, any friend of Mr. Bingley’s will always be welcome here, to be sure; but else I must say that I hate the very sight of him.” Jane looked at Elizabeth with surprise and concern.
BEG_SENT She knew but little of their meeting in Derbyshire, and therefore felt for the awkwardness which must attend her sister, in seeing him almost for the first time after receiving his explanatory letter.
BEG_SENT Both sisters were uncomfortable enough.
BEG_SENT Each felt for the other, and of course for themselves; and their mother talked on, of her dislike of Mr. Darcy, and her resolution to be civil to him only as Mr. Bingley’s friend, without being heard by either of them.
BEG_SENT But Elizabeth had sources of uneasiness which could not be suspected by Jane, to whom she had never yet had courage to shew Mrs. Gardiner’s letter, or to relate her own change of sentiment towards him.
BEG_SENT To Jane, he could be only a man whose proposals she had refused, and whose merit she had undervalued; but to her own more extensive information, he was the person to whom the whole family were indebted for the first of benefits, and whom she regarded herself with an interest, if not quite so tender, at least as reasonable and just as what Jane felt for Bingley.
BEG_SENT Her astonishment at his coming—at his coming to Netherfield, to Longbourn, and voluntarily seeking her again, was almost equal to what she had known on first witnessing his altered behaviour in Derbyshire.

BEG_SENT But she would not be secure.
BEG_SENT “Let me first see how he behaves,” said she; “it will then be early enough for expectation.” She sat intently at work, striving to be composed, and without daring to lift up her eyes, till anxious curiosity carried them to the face of her sister as the servant was approaching the door.
BEG_SENT Jane looked a little paler than usual, but more sedate than Elizabeth had expected.
BEG_SENT On the gentlemen’s appearing, her colour increased; yet she received them with tolerable ease, and with a propriety of behaviour equally free from any symptom of resentment or any unnecessary complaisance.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth said as little to either as civility would allow, and sat down again to her work, with an eagerness which it did not often command.
BEG_SENT She had ventured only one glance at Darcy.
BEG_SENT He looked serious, as usual; and, she thought, more as he had been used to look in Hertfordshire, than as she had seen him at Pemberley.
BEG_SENT But, perhaps he could not in her mother’s presence be what he was before her uncle and aunt.
BEG_SENT It was a painful, but not an improbable, conjecture.
BEG_SENT Bingley, she had likewise seen for an instant, and in that short period saw him looking both pleased and embarrassed.
BEG_SENT He was received by Mrs. Bennet with a degree of civility which made her two daughters ashamed, especially when contrasted with the cold and ceremonious politeness of her curtsey and address to his friend.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth, particularly, who knew that her mother owed to the latter the preservation of her favourite daughter from irremediable infamy, was hurt and distressed to a most painful degree by a distinction so ill applied.
BEG_SENT Darcy, after inquiring of her how Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner did, a question which she could not answer without confusion, said scarcely anything.
BEG_SENT He was not seated by her; perhaps that was the reason of his silence; but it had not been so in Derbyshire.
BEG_SENT There he had talked to her friends, when he could not to herself.
BEG_SENT But now several minutes elapsed without bringing the sound of his voice; and when occasionally, unable to resist the impulse of curiosity, she raised her eyes to his face, she as often found him looking at Jane as at herself, and frequently on no object but the ground.
BEG_SENT More thoughtfulness and less anxiety to please, than when they last met, were plainly expressed.
BEG_SENT She was disappointed, and angry with herself for being so.
BEG_SENT “Could I expect it to be otherwise!” said she.
BEG_SENT “Yet why did he come?” She was in no humour for conversation with anyone but himself; and to him she had hardly courage to speak.
BEG_SENT She inquired after his sister, but could do no more.
BEG_SENT “It is a long time, Mr. Bingley, since you went away,” said Mrs. Bennet.
BEG_SENT He readily agreed to it.
BEG_SENT “I began to be afraid you would never come back again.
BEG_SENT People _did_ say you meant to quit the place entirely at Michaelmas; but, however, I hope it is not true.
BEG_SENT A great many changes have happened in the neighbourhood, since you went away.
BEG_SENT Miss Lucas is married and settled.
BEG_SENT And one of my own daughters.
BEG_SENT I suppose you have heard of it; indeed, you must have seen it in the papers.
BEG_SENT It was in The Times and The Courier, I know; though it was not put in as it ought to be.
BEG_SENT It was only said, ‘Lately, George Wickham, Esq.

BEG_SENT It was my brother Gardiner’s drawing up too, and I wonder how he came to make such an awkward business of it.
BEG_SENT Did you see it?” Bingley replied that he did, and made his congratulations.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth dared not lift up her eyes.
BEG_SENT How Mr. Darcy looked, therefore, she could not tell.
BEG_SENT “It is a delightful thing, to be sure, to have a daughter well married,” continued her mother, “but at the same time, Mr. Bingley, it is very hard to have her taken such a way from me.

BEG_SENT His regiment is there; for I suppose you have heard of his leaving the ——shire, and of his being gone into the regulars.
BEG_SENT Thank Heaven!
BEG_SENT he has _some_ friends, though perhaps not so many as he deserves.” Elizabeth, who knew this to be levelled at Mr. Darcy, was in such misery of shame, that she could hardly keep her seat.
BEG_SENT It drew from her, however, the exertion of speaking, which nothing else had so effectually done before; and she asked Bingley whether he meant to make any stay in the country at present.
BEG_SENT A few weeks, he believed.
BEG_SENT “When you have killed all your own birds, Mr. Bingley,” said her mother, “I beg you will come here, and shoot as many as you please on Mr. Bennet’s manor.
BEG_SENT I am sure he will be vastly happy to oblige you, and will save all the best of the covies for you.” Elizabeth’s misery increased, at such unnecessary, such officious attention!
BEG_SENT Were the same fair prospect to arise at present as had flattered them a year ago, every thing, she was persuaded, would be hastening to the same vexatious conclusion.
BEG_SENT At that instant, she felt that years of happiness could not make Jane or herself amends for moments of such painful confusion.
BEG_SENT “The first wish of my heart,” said she to herself, “is never more to be in company with either of them.
BEG_SENT Their society can afford no pleasure that will atone for such wretchedness as this!
BEG_SENT Let me never see either one or the other again!” Yet the misery, for which years of happiness were to offer no compensation, received soon afterwards material relief, from observing how much the beauty of her sister re-kindled the admiration of her former lover.
BEG_SENT When first he came in, he had spoken to her but little; but every five minutes seemed to be giving her more of his attention.
BEG_SENT He found her as handsome as she had been last year; as good natured, and as unaffected, though not quite so chatty.
BEG_SENT Jane was anxious that no difference should be perceived in her at all, and was really persuaded that she talked as much as ever.
BEG_SENT But her mind was so busily engaged, that she did not always know when she was silent.
BEG_SENT When the gentlemen rose to go away, Mrs. Bennet was mindful of her intended civility, and they were invited and engaged to dine at Longbourn in a few days time.
BEG_SENT “You are quite a visit in my debt, Mr. Bingley,” she added, “for when you went to town last winter, you promised to take a family dinner with us, as soon as you returned.
BEG_SENT I have not forgot, you see; and I assure you, I was very much disappointed that you did not come back and keep your engagement.” Bingley looked a little silly at this reflection, and said something of his concern at having been prevented by business.
BEG_SENT They then went away.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet had been strongly inclined to ask them to stay and dine there that day; but, though she always kept a very good table, she did not think anything less than two courses could be good enough for a man on whom she had such anxious designs, or satisfy the appetite and pride of one who had ten thousand a year.
BEG_SENT   As soon as they were gone, Elizabeth walked out to recover her spirits; or in other words, to dwell without interruption on those subjects that must deaden them more.
BEG_SENT Mr. Darcy’s behaviour astonished and vexed her.
BEG_SENT “Why, if he came only to be silent, grave, and indifferent,” said she, “did he come at all?” She could settle it in no way that gave her pleasure.
BEG_SENT “He could be still amiable, still pleasing, to my uncle and aunt, when he was in town; and why not to me?
BEG_SENT If he fears me, why come hither?
BEG_SENT If he no longer cares for me, why silent?
BEG_SENT Teasing, teasing, man!
BEG_SENT I will think no more about him.” Her resolution was for a short time involuntarily kept by the approach of her sister, who joined her with a cheerful look, which showed her better satisfied with their visitors, than Elizabeth.
BEG_SENT “Now,” said she, “that this first meeting is over, I feel perfectly easy.
BEG_SENT I know my own strength, and I shall never be embarrassed again by his coming.
BEG_SENT I am glad he dines here on Tuesday.

BEG_SENT “Oh, Jane, take care.” “My dear Lizzy, you cannot think me so weak, as to be in danger now?” “I think you are in very great danger of making him as much in love with you as ever.” They did not see the gentlemen again till Tuesday; and Mrs. Bennet, in the meanwhile, was giving way to all the happy schemes, which the good humour and common politeness of Bingley, in half an hour’s visit, had revived.
BEG_SENT On Tuesday there was a large party assembled at Longbourn; and the two who were most anxiously expected, to the credit of their punctuality as sportsmen, were in very good time.
BEG_SENT When they repaired to the dining-room, Elizabeth eagerly watched to see whether Bingley would take the place, which, in all their former parties, had belonged to him, by her sister.
BEG_SENT Her prudent mother, occupied by the same ideas, forbore to invite him to sit by herself.
BEG_SENT On entering the room, he seemed to hesitate; but Jane happened to look round, and happened to smile: it was decided.
BEG_SENT He placed himself by her.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth, with a triumphant sensation, looked towards his friend.
BEG_SENT He bore it with noble indifference, and she would have imagined that Bingley had received his sanction to be happy, had she not seen his eyes likewise turned towards Mr. Darcy, with an expression of half-laughing alarm.
BEG_SENT His behaviour to her sister was such, during dinner time, as showed an admiration of her, which, though more guarded than formerly, persuaded Elizabeth, that if left wholly to himself, Jane’s happiness, and his own, would be speedily secured.
BEG_SENT Though she dared not depend upon the consequence, she yet received pleasure from observing his behaviour.
BEG_SENT It gave her all the animation that her spirits could boast; for she was in no cheerful humour.
BEG_SENT Mr. Darcy was almost as far from her as the table could divide them.
BEG_SENT He was on one side of her mother.
BEG_SENT She knew how little such a situation would give pleasure to either, or make either appear to advantage.
BEG_SENT She was not near enough to hear any of their discourse, but she could see how seldom they spoke to each other, and how formal and cold was their manner whenever they did.
BEG_SENT Her mother’s ungraciousness, made the sense of what they owed him more painful to Elizabeth’s mind; and she would, at times, have given anything to be privileged to tell him that his kindness was neither unknown nor unfelt by the whole of the family.
BEG_SENT She was in hopes that the evening would afford some opportunity of bringing them together; that the whole of the visit would not pass away without enabling them to enter into something more of conversation than the mere ceremonious salutation attending his entrance.
BEG_SENT Anxious and uneasy, the period which passed in the drawing-room, before the gentlemen came, was wearisome and dull to a degree that almost made her uncivil.
BEG_SENT She looked forward to their entrance as the point on which all her chance of pleasure for the evening must depend.
BEG_SENT “If he does not come to me, _then_,” said she, “I shall give him up for ever.” The gentlemen came; and she thought he looked as if he would have answered her hopes; but, alas!
BEG_SENT the ladies had crowded round the table, where Miss Bennet was making tea, and Elizabeth pouring out the coffee, in so close a confederacy that there was not a single vacancy near her which would admit of a chair.
BEG_SENT And on the gentlemen’s approaching, one of the girls moved closer to her than ever, and said, in a whisper: “The men shan’t come and part us, I am determined.
BEG_SENT We want none of them; do we?” Darcy had walked away to another part of the room.
BEG_SENT She followed him with her eyes, envied everyone to whom he spoke, had scarcely patience enough to help anybody to coffee; and then was enraged against herself for being so silly!
BEG_SENT “A man who has once been refused!
BEG_SENT How could I ever be foolish enough to expect a renewal of his love?
BEG_SENT Is there one among the sex, who would not protest against such a weakness as a second proposal to the same woman?

BEG_SENT Have all her friends left her?” “Mrs.
BEG_SENT Annesley is with her.
BEG_SENT The others have been gone on to Scarborough, these three weeks.” She could think of nothing more to say; but if he wished to converse with her, he might have better success.

BEG_SENT She now lost every expectation of pleasure.
BEG_SENT They were confined for the evening at different tables, and she had nothing to hope, but that his eyes were so often turned towards her side of the room, as to make him play as unsuccessfully as herself.

BEG_SENT The dinner was as well dressed as any I ever saw.
BEG_SENT The venison was roasted to a turn—and everybody said they never saw so fat a haunch.
BEG_SENT The soup was fifty times better than what we had at the Lucases’ last week; and even Mr. Darcy acknowledged, that the partridges were remarkably well done; and I suppose he has two or three French cooks at least.

BEG_SENT Mrs. Long said so too, for I asked her whether you did not.
BEG_SENT And what do you think she said besides?
BEG_SENT ‘Ah!
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet, we shall have her at Netherfield at last.’ She did indeed.

BEG_SENT “The party seemed so well selected, so suitable one with the other.
BEG_SENT I hope we may often meet again.” Elizabeth smiled.

BEG_SENT You must not suspect me.

BEG_SENT We all love to instruct, though we can teach only what is not worth knowing.
BEG_SENT Forgive me; and if you persist in indifference, do not make _me_ your confidante.”   A few days after this visit, Mr. Bingley called again, and alone.
BEG_SENT His friend had left him that morning for London, but was to return home in ten days time.
BEG_SENT He sat with them above an hour, and was in remarkably good spirits.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet invited him to dine with them; but, with many expressions of concern, he confessed himself engaged elsewhere.
BEG_SENT “Next time you call,” said she, “I hope we shall be more lucky.” He should be particularly happy at any time, etc.
BEG_SENT etc.
BEG_SENT ; and if she would give him leave, would take an early opportunity of waiting on them.
BEG_SENT “Can you come to-morrow?” Yes, he had no engagement at all for to-morrow; and her invitation was accepted with alacrity.
BEG_SENT He came, and in such very good time that the ladies were none of them dressed.

BEG_SENT He is come—Mr.
BEG_SENT Bingley is come.
BEG_SENT He is, indeed.
BEG_SENT Make haste, make haste.
BEG_SENT Here, Sarah, come to Miss Bennet this moment, and help her on with her gown.
BEG_SENT Never mind Miss Lizzy’s hair.” “We will be down as soon as we can,” said Jane; “but I dare say Kitty is forwarder than either of us, for she went up stairs half an hour ago.” “Oh!

BEG_SENT what has she to do with it?
BEG_SENT Come be quick, be quick!
BEG_SENT Where is your sash, my dear?” But when her mother was gone, Jane would not be prevailed on to go down without one of her sisters.
BEG_SENT The same anxiety to get them by themselves was visible again in the evening.
BEG_SENT After tea, Mr. Bennet retired to the library, as was his custom, and Mary went up stairs to her instrument.
BEG_SENT Two obstacles of the five being thus removed, Mrs. Bennet sat looking and winking at Elizabeth and Catherine for a considerable time, without making any impression on them.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth would not observe her; and when at last Kitty did, she very innocently said, “What is the matter mamma?
BEG_SENT What do you keep winking at me for?
BEG_SENT What am I to do?” “Nothing child, nothing.

BEG_SENT Jane instantly gave a look at Elizabeth which spoke her distress at such premeditation, and her entreaty that _she_ would not give in to it.
BEG_SENT In a few minutes, Mrs. Bennet half-opened the door and called out: “Lizzy, my dear, I want to speak with you.” Elizabeth was forced to go.
BEG_SENT “We may as well leave them by themselves you know;” said her mother, as soon as she was in the hall.
BEG_SENT “Kitty and I are going up stairs to sit in my dressing-room.” Elizabeth made no attempt to reason with her mother, but remained quietly in the hall, till she and Kitty were out of sight, then returned into the drawing-room.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet’s schemes for this day were ineffectual.
BEG_SENT Bingley was every thing that was charming, except the professed lover of her daughter.
BEG_SENT His ease and cheerfulness rendered him a most agreeable addition to their evening party; and he bore with the ill-judged officiousness of the mother, and heard all her silly remarks with a forbearance and command of countenance particularly grateful to the daughter.
BEG_SENT He scarcely needed an invitation to stay supper; and before he went away, an engagement was formed, chiefly through his own and Mrs. Bennet’s means, for his coming next morning to shoot with her husband.
BEG_SENT After this day, Jane said no more of her indifference.
BEG_SENT Not a word passed between the sisters concerning Bingley; but Elizabeth went to bed in the happy belief that all must speedily be concluded, unless Mr. Darcy returned within the stated time.
BEG_SENT Seriously, however, she felt tolerably persuaded that all this must have taken place with that gentleman’s concurrence.
BEG_SENT Bingley was punctual to his appointment; and he and Mr. Bennet spent the morning together, as had been agreed on.
BEG_SENT The latter was much more agreeable than his companion expected.
BEG_SENT There was nothing of presumption or folly in Bingley that could provoke his ridicule, or disgust him into silence; and he was more communicative, and less eccentric, than the other had ever seen him.
BEG_SENT Bingley of course returned with him to dinner; and in the evening Mrs. Bennet’s invention was again at work to get every body away from him and her daughter.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth, who had a letter to write, went into the breakfast room for that purpose soon after tea; for as the others were all going to sit down to cards, she could not be wanted to counteract her mother’s schemes.
BEG_SENT But on returning to the drawing-room, when her letter was finished, she saw, to her infinite surprise, there was reason to fear that her mother had been too ingenious for her.

BEG_SENT I do not deserve it.
BEG_SENT Oh!

BEG_SENT Every sentence of kindness was a fresh source of happiness to Jane.

BEG_SENT Oh!
BEG_SENT Lizzy, to know that what I have to relate will give such pleasure to all my dear family!

BEG_SENT the happiest, wisest, most reasonable end!” In a few minutes she was joined by Bingley, whose conference with her father had been short and to the purpose.

BEG_SENT She will be down in a moment, I dare say.” He then shut the door, and, coming up to her, claimed the good wishes and affection of a sister.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth honestly and heartily expressed her delight in the prospect of their relationship.

BEG_SENT It was an evening of no common delight to them all; the satisfaction of Miss Bennet’s mind gave a glow of such sweet animation to her face, as made her look handsomer than ever.
BEG_SENT Kitty simpered and smiled, and hoped her turn was coming soon.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet could not give her consent or speak her approbation in terms warm enough to satisfy her feelings, though she talked to Bingley of nothing else for half an hour; and when Mr. Bennet joined them at supper, his voice and manner plainly showed how really happy he was.

BEG_SENT You will be a very happy woman.” Jane went to him instantly, kissed him, and thanked him for his goodness.
BEG_SENT “You are a good girl;” he replied, “and I have great pleasure in thinking you will be so happily settled.
BEG_SENT I have not a doubt of your doing very well together.
BEG_SENT Your tempers are by no means unlike.

BEG_SENT Oh!
BEG_SENT he is the handsomest young man that ever was seen!” Wickham, Lydia, were all forgotten.
BEG_SENT Jane was beyond competition her favourite child.
BEG_SENT At that moment, she cared for no other.
BEG_SENT Her younger sisters soon began to make interest with her for objects of happiness which she might in future be able to dispense.
BEG_SENT Mary petitioned for the use of the library at Netherfield; and Kitty begged very hard for a few balls there every winter.

BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet was privileged to whisper it to Mrs. Phillips, and she ventured, without any permission, to do the same by all her neighbours in Meryton.
BEG_SENT The Bennets were speedily pronounced to be the luckiest family in the world, though only a few weeks before, when Lydia had first run away, they had been generally proved to be marked out for misfortune.
BEG_SENT   One morning, about a week after Bingley’s engagement with Jane had been formed, as he and the females of the family were sitting together in the dining-room, their attention was suddenly drawn to the window, by the sound of a carriage; and they perceived a chaise and four driving up the lawn.
BEG_SENT It was too early in the morning for visitors, and besides, the equipage did not answer to that of any of their neighbours.
BEG_SENT The horses were post; and neither the carriage, nor the livery of the servant who preceded it, were familiar to them.
BEG_SENT As it was certain, however, that somebody was coming, Bingley instantly prevailed on Miss Bennet to avoid the confinement of such an intrusion, and walk away with him into the shrubbery.
BEG_SENT They both set off, and the conjectures of the remaining three continued, though with little satisfaction, till the door was thrown open and their visitor entered.
BEG_SENT It was Lady Catherine de Bourgh.
BEG_SENT They were of course all intending to be surprised; but their astonishment was beyond their expectation; and on the part of Mrs. Bennet and Kitty, though she was perfectly unknown to them, even inferior to what Elizabeth felt.
BEG_SENT She entered the room with an air more than usually ungracious, made no other reply to Elizabeth’s salutation than a slight inclination of the head, and sat down without saying a word.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth had mentioned her name to her mother on her ladyship’s entrance, though no request of introduction had been made.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet, all amazement, though flattered by having a guest of such high importance, received her with the utmost politeness.

BEG_SENT “And _that_ I suppose is one of your sisters.” “Yes, madam,” said Mrs. Bennet, delighted to speak to Lady Catherine.

BEG_SENT My youngest of all is lately married, and my eldest is somewhere about the grounds, walking with a young man who, I believe, will soon become a part of the family.” “You have a very small park here,” returned Lady Catherine after a short silence.
BEG_SENT “It is nothing in comparison of Rosings, my lady, I dare say; but I assure you it is much larger than Sir William Lucas’s.” “This must be a most inconvenient sitting room for the evening, in summer; the windows are full west.” Mrs. Bennet assured her that they never sat there after dinner, and then added: “May I take the liberty of asking your ladyship whether you left Mr. and Mrs. Collins well.” “Yes, very well.

BEG_SENT But no letter appeared, and she was completely puzzled.

BEG_SENT Mr. Darcy is engaged to _my daughter_.

BEG_SENT From their infancy, they have been intended for each other.
BEG_SENT It was the favourite wish of _his_ mother, as well as of hers.

BEG_SENT My daughter and my nephew are formed for each other.
