BEG_SENT But who was your mother?
BEG_SENT Who are your uncles and aunts?
BEG_SENT Do not imagine me ignorant of their condition.” “Whatever my connections may be,” said Elizabeth, “if your nephew does not object to them, they can be nothing to _you_.” “Tell me once for all, are you engaged to him?” Though Elizabeth would not, for the mere purpose of obliging Lady Catherine, have answered this question, she could not but say, after a moment’s deliberation: “I am not.” Lady Catherine seemed pleased.
BEG_SENT “And will you promise me, never to enter into such an engagement?” “I will make no promise of the kind.” “Miss Bennet I am shocked and astonished.
BEG_SENT I expected to find a more reasonable young woman.
BEG_SENT But do not deceive yourself into a belief that I will ever recede.
BEG_SENT I shall not go away till you have given me the assurance I require.” “And I certainly _never_ shall give it.
BEG_SENT I am not to be intimidated into anything so wholly unreasonable.
BEG_SENT Your ladyship wants Mr. Darcy to marry your daughter; but would my giving you the wished-for promise make _their_ marriage at all more probable?
BEG_SENT Supposing him to be attached to me, would _my_ refusing to accept his hand make him wish to bestow it on his cousin?
BEG_SENT Allow me to say, Lady Catherine, that the arguments with which you have supported this extraordinary application have been as frivolous as the application was ill-judged.
BEG_SENT You have widely mistaken my character, if you think I can be worked on by such persuasions as these.
BEG_SENT How far your nephew might approve of your interference in _his_ affairs, I cannot tell; but you have certainly no right to concern yourself in mine.
BEG_SENT I must beg, therefore, to be importuned no farther on the subject.” “Not so hasty, if you please.
BEG_SENT I have by no means done.
BEG_SENT To all the objections I have already urged, I have still another to add.
BEG_SENT I am no stranger to the particulars of your youngest sister’s infamous elopement.
BEG_SENT I know it all; that the young man’s marrying her was a patched-up business, at the expence of your father and uncles.
BEG_SENT And is _such_ a girl to be my nephew’s sister?
BEG_SENT Is _her_ husband, who is the son of his late father’s steward, to be his brother?
BEG_SENT Heaven and earth!—of what are you thinking?
BEG_SENT Are the shades of Pemberley to be thus polluted?” “You can _now_ have nothing further to say,” she resentfully answered.
BEG_SENT “You have insulted me in every possible method.
BEG_SENT I must beg to return to the house.” And she rose as she spoke.
BEG_SENT Lady Catherine rose also, and they turned back.
BEG_SENT Her ladyship was highly incensed.
BEG_SENT “You have no regard, then, for the honour and credit of my nephew!
BEG_SENT Unfeeling, selfish girl!
BEG_SENT Do you not consider that a connection with you must disgrace him in the eyes of everybody?” “Lady Catherine, I have nothing further to say.
BEG_SENT You know my sentiments.” “You are then resolved to have him?” “I have said no such thing.
BEG_SENT I am only resolved to act in that manner, which will, in my own opinion, constitute my happiness, without reference to _you_, or to any person so wholly unconnected with me.” “It is well.
BEG_SENT You refuse, then, to oblige me.
BEG_SENT You refuse to obey the claims of duty, honour, and gratitude.
BEG_SENT You are determined to ruin him in the opinion of all his friends, and make him the contempt of the world.” “Neither duty, nor honour, nor gratitude,” replied Elizabeth, “have any possible claim on me, in the present instance.
BEG_SENT No principle of either would be violated by my marriage with Mr. Darcy.
BEG_SENT And with regard to the resentment of his family, or the indignation of the world, if the former _were_ excited by his marrying me, it would not give me one moment’s concern—and the world in general would have too much sense to join in the scorn.” “And this is your real opinion!
BEG_SENT This is your final resolve!
BEG_SENT Very well.
BEG_SENT I shall now know how to act.
BEG_SENT Do not imagine, Miss Bennet, that your ambition will ever be gratified.
BEG_SENT I came to try you.
BEG_SENT I hoped to find you reasonable; but, depend upon it, I will carry my point.” In this manner Lady Catherine talked on, till they were at the door of the carriage, when, turning hastily round, she added, “I take no leave of you, Miss Bennet.
BEG_SENT I send no compliments to your mother.
BEG_SENT You deserve no such attention.
BEG_SENT I am most seriously displeased.” Elizabeth made no answer; and without attempting to persuade her ladyship to return into the house, walked quietly into it herself.
BEG_SENT She heard the carriage drive away as she proceeded up stairs.
BEG_SENT Her mother impatiently met her at the door of the dressing-room, to ask why Lady Catherine would not come in again and rest herself.
BEG_SENT “She did not choose it,” said her daughter, “she would go.” “She is a very fine-looking woman!
BEG_SENT and her calling here was prodigiously civil!
BEG_SENT for she only came, I suppose, to tell us the Collinses were well.
BEG_SENT She is on her road somewhere, I dare say, and so, passing through Meryton, thought she might as well call on you.
BEG_SENT I suppose she had nothing particular to say to you, Lizzy?” Elizabeth was forced to give into a little falsehood here; for to acknowledge the substance of their conversation was impossible.
BEG_SENT   The discomposure of spirits which this extraordinary visit threw Elizabeth into, could not be easily overcome; nor could she, for many hours, learn to think of it less than incessantly.
BEG_SENT Lady Catherine, it appeared, had actually taken the trouble of this journey from Rosings, for the sole purpose of breaking off her supposed engagement with Mr. Darcy.
BEG_SENT It was a rational scheme, to be sure!
BEG_SENT but from what the report of their engagement could originate, Elizabeth was at a loss to imagine; till she recollected that _his_ being the intimate friend of Bingley, and _her_ being the sister of Jane, was enough, at a time when the expectation of one wedding made everybody eager for another, to supply the idea.
BEG_SENT She had not herself forgotten to feel that the marriage of her sister must bring them more frequently together.
BEG_SENT And her neighbours at Lucas Lodge, therefore (for through their communication with the Collinses, the report, she concluded, had reached Lady Catherine), had only set _that_ down as almost certain and immediate, which _she_ had looked forward to as possible at some future time.
BEG_SENT In revolving Lady Catherine’s expressions, however, she could not help feeling some uneasiness as to the possible consequence of her persisting in this interference.
BEG_SENT From what she had said of her resolution to prevent their marriage, it occurred to Elizabeth that she must meditate an application to her nephew; and how he might take a similar representation of the evils attached to a connection with her, she dared not pronounce.
BEG_SENT She knew not the exact degree of his affection for his aunt, or his dependence on her judgment, but it was natural to suppose that he thought much higher of her ladyship than _she_ could do; and it was certain that, in enumerating the miseries of a marriage with _one_, whose immediate connections were so unequal to his own, his aunt would address him on his weakest side.
BEG_SENT With his notions of dignity, he would probably feel that the arguments, which to Elizabeth had appeared weak and ridiculous, contained much good sense and solid reasoning.
BEG_SENT If he had been wavering before as to what he should do, which had often seemed likely, the advice and entreaty of so near a relation might settle every doubt, and determine him at once to be as happy as dignity unblemished could make him.
BEG_SENT In that case he would return no more.
BEG_SENT Lady Catherine might see him in her way through town; and his engagement to Bingley of coming again to Netherfield must give way.
BEG_SENT “If, therefore, an excuse for not keeping his promise should come to his friend within a few days,” she added, “I shall know how to understand it.
BEG_SENT I shall then give over every expectation, every wish of his constancy.
BEG_SENT If he is satisfied with only regretting me, when he might have obtained my affections and hand, I shall soon cease to regret him at all.” The surprise of the rest of the family, on hearing who their visitor had been, was very great; but they obligingly satisfied it, with the same kind of supposition which had appeased Mrs. Bennet’s curiosity; and Elizabeth was spared from much teasing on the subject.
BEG_SENT The next morning, as she was going downstairs, she was met by her father, who came out of his library with a letter in his hand.
BEG_SENT “Lizzy,” said he, “I was going to look for you; come into my room.” She followed him thither; and her curiosity to know what he had to tell her was heightened by the supposition of its being in some manner connected with the letter he held.
BEG_SENT It suddenly struck her that it might be from Lady Catherine; and she anticipated with dismay all the consequent explanations.
BEG_SENT She followed her father to the fire place, and they both sat down.
BEG_SENT He then said, “I have received a letter this morning that has astonished me exceedingly.
BEG_SENT As it principally concerns yourself, you ought to know its contents.
BEG_SENT I did not know before, that I had _two_ daughters on the brink of matrimony.
BEG_SENT Let me congratulate you on a very important conquest.” The colour now rushed into Elizabeth’s cheeks in the instantaneous conviction of its being a letter from the nephew, instead of the aunt; and she was undetermined whether most to be pleased that he explained himself at all, or offended that his letter was not rather addressed to herself; when her father continued: “You look conscious.
BEG_SENT Young ladies have great penetration in such matters as these; but I think I may defy even _your_ sagacity, to discover the name of your admirer.
BEG_SENT This letter is from Mr. Collins.” “From Mr. Collins!
BEG_SENT and what can _he_ have to say?” “Something very much to the purpose of course.
BEG_SENT He begins with congratulations on the approaching nuptials of my eldest daughter, of which, it seems, he has been told by some of the good-natured, gossiping Lucases.
BEG_SENT I shall not sport with your impatience, by reading what he says on that point.
BEG_SENT What relates to yourself, is as follows: ‘Having thus offered you the sincere congratulations of Mrs. Collins and myself on this happy event, let me now add a short hint on the subject of another; of which we have been advertised by the same authority.
BEG_SENT Your daughter Elizabeth, it is presumed, will not long bear the name of Bennet, after her elder sister has resigned it, and the chosen partner of her fate may be reasonably looked up to as one of the most illustrious personages in this land.’ “Can you possibly guess, Lizzy, who is meant by this?
BEG_SENT ‘This young gentleman is blessed, in a peculiar way, with every thing the heart of mortal can most desire,—splendid property, noble kindred, and extensive patronage.
BEG_SENT Yet in spite of all these temptations, let me warn my cousin Elizabeth, and yourself, of what evils you may incur by a precipitate closure with this gentleman’s proposals, which, of course, you will be inclined to take immediate advantage of.’ “Have you any idea, Lizzy, who this gentleman is?
BEG_SENT But now it comes out: “‘My motive for cautioning you is as follows.
BEG_SENT We have reason to imagine that his aunt, Lady Catherine de Bourgh, does not look on the match with a friendly eye.’ “_Mr.
BEG_SENT Darcy_, you see, is the man!
BEG_SENT Now, Lizzy, I think I _have_ surprised you.
BEG_SENT Could he, or the Lucases, have pitched on any man within the circle of our acquaintance, whose name would have given the lie more effectually to what they related?
BEG_SENT Mr. Darcy, who never looks at any woman but to see a blemish, and who probably never looked at _you_ in his life!
BEG_SENT It is admirable!” Elizabeth tried to join in her father’s pleasantry, but could only force one most reluctant smile.
BEG_SENT Never had his wit been directed in a manner so little agreeable to her.
BEG_SENT “Are you not diverted?” “Oh!
BEG_SENT yes.
BEG_SENT Pray read on.” “‘After mentioning the likelihood of this marriage to her ladyship last night, she immediately, with her usual condescension, expressed what she felt on the occasion; when it became apparent, that on the score of some family objections on the part of my cousin, she would never give her consent to what she termed so disgraceful a match.
BEG_SENT I thought it my duty to give the speediest intelligence of this to my cousin, that she and her noble admirer may be aware of what they are about, and not run hastily into a marriage which has not been properly sanctioned.’ Mr. Collins moreover adds, ‘I am truly rejoiced that my cousin Lydia’s sad business has been so well hushed up, and am only concerned that their living together before the marriage took place should be so generally known.
BEG_SENT I must not, however, neglect the duties of my station, or refrain from declaring my amazement at hearing that you received the young couple into your house as soon as they were married.
BEG_SENT It was an encouragement of vice; and had I been the rector of Longbourn, I should very strenuously have opposed it.
BEG_SENT You ought certainly to forgive them, as a Christian, but never to admit them in your sight, or allow their names to be mentioned in your hearing.’ _That_ is his notion of Christian forgiveness!
BEG_SENT The rest of his letter is only about his dear Charlotte’s situation, and his expectation of a young olive-branch.
BEG_SENT But, Lizzy, you look as if you did not enjoy it.
BEG_SENT You are not going to be _missish_, I hope, and pretend to be affronted at an idle report.
BEG_SENT For what do we live, but to make sport for our neighbours, and laugh at them in our turn?” “Oh!” cried Elizabeth, “I am excessively diverted.
BEG_SENT But it is so strange!” “Yes—_that_ is what makes it amusing.
BEG_SENT Had they fixed on any other man it would have been nothing; but _his_ perfect indifference, and _your_ pointed dislike, make it so delightfully absurd!
BEG_SENT Much as I abominate writing, I would not give up Mr. Collins’s correspondence for any consideration.
BEG_SENT Nay, when I read a letter of his, I cannot help giving him the preference even over Wickham, much as I value the impudence and hypocrisy of my son-in-law.
BEG_SENT And pray, Lizzy, what said Lady Catherine about this report?
BEG_SENT Did she call to refuse her consent?” To this question his daughter replied only with a laugh; and as it had been asked without the least suspicion, she was not distressed by his repeating it.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth had never been more at a loss to make her feelings appear what they were not.
BEG_SENT It was necessary to laugh, when she would rather have cried.
BEG_SENT Her father had most cruelly mortified her, by what he said of Mr. Darcy’s indifference, and she could do nothing but wonder at such a want of penetration, or fear that perhaps, instead of his seeing too _little_, she might have fancied too _much_.
BEG_SENT   Instead of receiving any such letter of excuse from his friend, as Elizabeth half expected Mr. Bingley to do, he was able to bring Darcy with him to Longbourn before many days had passed after Lady Catherine’s visit.
BEG_SENT The gentlemen arrived early; and, before Mrs. Bennet had time to tell him of their having seen his aunt, of which her daughter sat in momentary dread, Bingley, who wanted to be alone with Jane, proposed their all walking out.
BEG_SENT It was agreed to.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Bennet was not in the habit of walking; Mary could never spare time; but the remaining five set off together.
BEG_SENT Bingley and Jane, however, soon allowed the others to outstrip them.
BEG_SENT They lagged behind, while Elizabeth, Kitty, and Darcy were to entertain each other.
BEG_SENT Very little was said by either; Kitty was too much afraid of him to talk; Elizabeth was secretly forming a desperate resolution; and perhaps he might be doing the same.
BEG_SENT They walked towards the Lucases, because Kitty wished to call upon Maria; and as Elizabeth saw no occasion for making it a general concern, when Kitty left them she went boldly on with him alone.
BEG_SENT Now was the moment for her resolution to be executed, and, while her courage was high, she immediately said: “Mr.
BEG_SENT Darcy, I am a very selfish creature; and, for the sake of giving relief to my own feelings, care not how much I may be wounding yours.
BEG_SENT I can no longer help thanking you for your unexampled kindness to my poor sister.
BEG_SENT Ever since I have known it, I have been most anxious to acknowledge to you how gratefully I feel it.
BEG_SENT Were it known to the rest of my family, I should not have merely my own gratitude to express.” “I am sorry, exceedingly sorry,” replied Darcy, in a tone of surprise and emotion, “that you have ever been informed of what may, in a mistaken light, have given you uneasiness.
BEG_SENT I did not think Mrs. Gardiner was so little to be trusted.” “You must not blame my aunt.
BEG_SENT Lydia’s thoughtlessness first betrayed to me that you had been concerned in the matter; and, of course, I could not rest till I knew the particulars.
BEG_SENT Let me thank you again and again, in the name of all my family, for that generous compassion which induced you to take so much trouble, and bear so many mortifications, for the sake of discovering them.” “If you _will_ thank me,” he replied, “let it be for yourself alone.
BEG_SENT That the wish of giving happiness to you might add force to the other inducements which led me on, I shall not attempt to deny.
BEG_SENT But your _family_ owe me nothing.
BEG_SENT Much as I respect them, I believe I thought only of _you_.” Elizabeth was too much embarrassed to say a word.
BEG_SENT After a short pause, her companion added, “You are too generous to trifle with me.
BEG_SENT If your feelings are still what they were last April, tell me so at once.
BEG_SENT _My_ affections and wishes are unchanged, but one word from you will silence me on this subject for ever.” Elizabeth, feeling all the more than common awkwardness and anxiety of his situation, now forced herself to speak; and immediately, though not very fluently, gave him to understand that her sentiments had undergone so material a change, since the period to which he alluded, as to make her receive with gratitude and pleasure his present assurances.
BEG_SENT The happiness which this reply produced, was such as he had probably never felt before; and he expressed himself on the occasion as sensibly and as warmly as a man violently in love can be supposed to do.
BEG_SENT Had Elizabeth been able to encounter his eye, she might have seen how well the expression of heartfelt delight, diffused over his face, became him; but, though she could not look, she could listen, and he told her of feelings, which, in proving of what importance she was to him, made his affection every moment more valuable.
BEG_SENT They walked on, without knowing in what direction.
BEG_SENT There was too much to be thought, and felt, and said, for attention to any other objects.
BEG_SENT She soon learnt that they were indebted for their present good understanding to the efforts of his aunt, who _did_ call on him in her return through London, and there relate her journey to Longbourn, its motive, and the substance of her conversation with Elizabeth; dwelling emphatically on every expression of the latter which, in her ladyship’s apprehension, peculiarly denoted her perverseness and assurance; in the belief that such a relation must assist her endeavours to obtain that promise from her nephew which _she_ had refused to give.
BEG_SENT But, unluckily for her ladyship, its effect had been exactly contrariwise.
BEG_SENT “It taught me to hope,” said he, “as I had scarcely ever allowed myself to hope before.
BEG_SENT I knew enough of your disposition to be certain that, had you been absolutely, irrevocably decided against me, you would have acknowledged it to Lady Catherine, frankly and openly.” Elizabeth coloured and laughed as she replied, “Yes, you know enough of my _frankness_ to believe me capable of _that_.
BEG_SENT After abusing you so abominably to your face, I could have no scruple in abusing you to all your relations.” “What did you say of me, that I did not deserve?
BEG_SENT For, though your accusations were ill-founded, formed on mistaken premises, my behaviour to you at the time had merited the severest reproof.
BEG_SENT It was unpardonable.
BEG_SENT I cannot think of it without abhorrence.” “We will not quarrel for the greater share of blame annexed to that evening,” said Elizabeth.
BEG_SENT “The conduct of neither, if strictly examined, will be irreproachable; but since then, we have both, I hope, improved in civility.” “I cannot be so easily reconciled to myself.
BEG_SENT The recollection of what I then said, of my conduct, my manners, my expressions during the whole of it, is now, and has been many months, inexpressibly painful to me.
BEG_SENT Your reproof, so well applied, I shall never forget: ‘had you behaved in a more gentlemanlike manner.’ Those were your words.
BEG_SENT You know not, you can scarcely conceive, how they have tortured me;—though it was some time, I confess, before I was reasonable enough to allow their justice.” “I was certainly very far from expecting them to make so strong an impression.
BEG_SENT I had not the smallest idea of their being ever felt in such a way.” “I can easily believe it.
BEG_SENT You thought me then devoid of every proper feeling, I am sure you did.
BEG_SENT The turn of your countenance I shall never forget, as you said that I could not have addressed you in any possible way that would induce you to accept me.” “Oh!
BEG_SENT do not repeat what I then said.
BEG_SENT These recollections will not do at all.
BEG_SENT I assure you that I have long been most heartily ashamed of it.” Darcy mentioned his letter.
BEG_SENT “Did it,” said he, “did it soon make you think better of me?
BEG_SENT Did you, on reading it, give any credit to its contents?” She explained what its effect on her had been, and how gradually all her former prejudices had been removed.
BEG_SENT “I knew,” said he, “that what I wrote must give you pain, but it was necessary.
BEG_SENT I hope you have destroyed the letter.
BEG_SENT There was one part especially, the opening of it, which I should dread your having the power of reading again.
BEG_SENT I can remember some expressions which might justly make you hate me.” “The letter shall certainly be burnt, if you believe it essential to the preservation of my regard; but, though we have both reason to think my opinions not entirely unalterable, they are not, I hope, quite so easily changed as that implies.” “When I wrote that letter,” replied Darcy, “I believed myself perfectly calm and cool, but I am since convinced that it was written in a dreadful bitterness of spirit.” “The letter, perhaps, began in bitterness, but it did not end so.
BEG_SENT The adieu is charity itself.
BEG_SENT But think no more of the letter.
BEG_SENT The feelings of the person who wrote, and the person who received it, are now so widely different from what they were then, that every unpleasant circumstance attending it ought to be forgotten.
BEG_SENT You must learn some of my philosophy.
BEG_SENT Think only of the past as its remembrance gives you pleasure.” “I cannot give you credit for any philosophy of the kind.
BEG_SENT _Your_ retrospections must be so totally void of reproach, that the contentment arising from them is not of philosophy, but, what is much better, of innocence.
BEG_SENT But with _me_, it is not so.
BEG_SENT Painful recollections will intrude which cannot, which ought not, to be repelled.
BEG_SENT I have been a selfish being all my life, in practice, though not in principle.
BEG_SENT As a child I was taught what was _right_, but I was not taught to correct my temper.
BEG_SENT I was given good principles, but left to follow them in pride and conceit.
BEG_SENT Unfortunately an only son (for many years an only _child_), I was spoilt by my parents, who, though good themselves (my father, particularly, all that was benevolent and amiable), allowed, encouraged, almost taught me to be selfish and overbearing; to care for none beyond my own family circle; to think meanly of all the rest of the world; to _wish_ at least to think meanly of their sense and worth compared with my own.
BEG_SENT Such I was, from eight to eight and twenty; and such I might still have been but for you, dearest, loveliest Elizabeth!
BEG_SENT What do I not owe you!
BEG_SENT You taught me a lesson, hard indeed at first, but most advantageous.
BEG_SENT By you, I was properly humbled.
BEG_SENT I came to you without a doubt of my reception.
BEG_SENT You showed me how insufficient were all my pretensions to please a woman worthy of being pleased.” “Had you then persuaded yourself that I should?” “Indeed I had.
BEG_SENT What will you think of my vanity?
BEG_SENT I believed you to be wishing, expecting my addresses.” “My manners must have been in fault, but not intentionally, I assure you.
BEG_SENT I never meant to deceive you, but my spirits might often lead me wrong.
BEG_SENT How you must have hated me after _that_ evening?” “Hate you!
BEG_SENT I was angry perhaps at first, but my anger soon began to take a proper direction.” “I am almost afraid of asking what you thought of me, when we met at Pemberley.
BEG_SENT You blamed me for coming?” “No indeed; I felt nothing but surprise.” “Your surprise could not be greater than _mine_ in being noticed by you.
BEG_SENT My conscience told me that I deserved no extraordinary politeness, and I confess that I did not expect to receive _more_ than my due.” “My object _then_,” replied Darcy, “was to show you, by every civility in my power, that I was not so mean as to resent the past; and I hoped to obtain your forgiveness, to lessen your ill opinion, by letting you see that your reproofs had been attended to.
BEG_SENT How soon any other wishes introduced themselves I can hardly tell, but I believe in about half an hour after I had seen you.” He then told her of Georgiana’s delight in her acquaintance, and of her disappointment at its sudden interruption; which naturally leading to the cause of that interruption, she soon learnt that his resolution of following her from Derbyshire in quest of her sister had been formed before he quitted the inn, and that his gravity and thoughtfulness there had arisen from no other struggles than what such a purpose must comprehend.
BEG_SENT She expressed her gratitude again, but it was too painful a subject to each, to be dwelt on farther.
BEG_SENT After walking several miles in a leisurely manner, and too busy to know anything about it, they found at last, on examining their watches, that it was time to be at home.
BEG_SENT “What could become of Mr. Bingley and Jane!” was a wonder which introduced the discussion of _their_ affairs.
BEG_SENT Darcy was delighted with their engagement; his friend had given him the earliest information of it.
BEG_SENT “I must ask whether you were surprised?” said Elizabeth.
BEG_SENT “Not at all.
BEG_SENT When I went away, I felt that it would soon happen.” “That is to say, you had given your permission.
BEG_SENT I guessed as much.” And though he exclaimed at the term, she found that it had been pretty much the case.
BEG_SENT “On the evening before my going to London,” said he, “I made a confession to him, which I believe I ought to have made long ago.
BEG_SENT I told him of all that had occurred to make my former interference in his affairs absurd and impertinent.
BEG_SENT His surprise was great.
BEG_SENT He had never had the slightest suspicion.
BEG_SENT I told him, moreover, that I believed myself mistaken in supposing, as I had done, that your sister was indifferent to him; and as I could easily perceive that his attachment to her was unabated, I felt no doubt of their happiness together.” Elizabeth could not help smiling at his easy manner of directing his friend.
BEG_SENT “Did you speak from your own observation,” said she, “when you told him that my sister loved him, or merely from my information last spring?” “From the former.
BEG_SENT I had narrowly observed her during the two visits which I had lately made here; and I was convinced of her affection.” “And your assurance of it, I suppose, carried immediate conviction to him.” “It did.
BEG_SENT Bingley is most unaffectedly modest.
BEG_SENT His diffidence had prevented his depending on his own judgment in so anxious a case, but his reliance on mine made every thing easy.
BEG_SENT I was obliged to confess one thing, which for a time, and not unjustly, offended him.
BEG_SENT I could not allow myself to conceal that your sister had been in town three months last winter, that I had known it, and purposely kept it from him.
BEG_SENT He was angry.
BEG_SENT But his anger, I am persuaded, lasted no longer than he remained in any doubt of your sister’s sentiments.
BEG_SENT He has heartily forgiven me now.” Elizabeth longed to observe that Mr. Bingley had been a most delightful friend; so easily guided that his worth was invaluable; but she checked herself.
BEG_SENT She remembered that he had yet to learn to be laughed at, and it was rather too early to begin.
BEG_SENT In anticipating the happiness of Bingley, which of course was to be inferior only to his own, he continued the conversation till they reached the house.
BEG_SENT In the hall they parted.
BEG_SENT   “My dear Lizzy, where can you have been walking to?” was a question which Elizabeth received from Jane as soon as she entered their room, and from all the others when they sat down to table.
BEG_SENT She had only to say in reply, that they had wandered about, till she was beyond her own knowledge.
BEG_SENT She coloured as she spoke; but neither that, nor anything else, awakened a suspicion of the truth.
BEG_SENT The evening passed quietly, unmarked by anything extraordinary.
BEG_SENT The acknowledged lovers talked and laughed, the unacknowledged were silent.
BEG_SENT Darcy was not of a disposition in which happiness overflows in mirth; and Elizabeth, agitated and confused, rather _knew_ that she was happy than _felt_ herself to be so; for, besides the immediate embarrassment, there were other evils before her.
BEG_SENT She anticipated what would be felt in the family when her situation became known; she was aware that no one liked him but Jane; and even feared that with the others it was a _dislike_ which not all his fortune and consequence might do away.
BEG_SENT At night she opened her heart to Jane.
BEG_SENT Though suspicion was very far from Miss Bennet’s general habits, she was absolutely incredulous here.
BEG_SENT “You are joking, Lizzy.
BEG_SENT This cannot be!—engaged to Mr. Darcy!
BEG_SENT No, no, you shall not deceive me.
BEG_SENT I know it to be impossible.” “This is a wretched beginning indeed!
BEG_SENT My sole dependence was on you; and I am sure nobody else will believe me, if you do not.
BEG_SENT Yet, indeed, I am in earnest.
BEG_SENT I speak nothing but the truth.
BEG_SENT He still loves me, and we are engaged.” Jane looked at her doubtingly.
BEG_SENT “Oh, Lizzy!
BEG_SENT it cannot be.
BEG_SENT I know how much you dislike him.” “You know nothing of the matter.
BEG_SENT _That_ is all to be forgot.
BEG_SENT Perhaps I did not always love him so well as I do now.
BEG_SENT But in such cases as these, a good memory is unpardonable.
BEG_SENT This is the last time I shall ever remember it myself.” Miss Bennet still looked all amazement.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth again, and more seriously assured her of its truth.
BEG_SENT “Good Heaven!
BEG_SENT can it be really so!
BEG_SENT Yet now I must believe you,” cried Jane.
BEG_SENT “My dear, dear Lizzy, I would—I do congratulate you—but are you certain?
BEG_SENT forgive the question—are you quite certain that you can be happy with him?” “There can be no doubt of that.
BEG_SENT It is settled between us already, that we are to be the happiest couple in the world.
BEG_SENT But are you pleased, Jane?
BEG_SENT Shall you like to have such a brother?” “Very, very much.
BEG_SENT Nothing could give either Bingley or myself more delight.
BEG_SENT But we considered it, we talked of it as impossible.
BEG_SENT And do you really love him quite well enough?
BEG_SENT Oh, Lizzy!
BEG_SENT do anything rather than marry without affection.
BEG_SENT Are you quite sure that you feel what you ought to do?” “Oh, yes!
BEG_SENT You will only think I feel _more_ than I ought to do, when I tell you all.” “What do you mean?” “Why, I must confess that I love him better than I do Bingley.
BEG_SENT I am afraid you will be angry.” “My dearest sister, now _be_ serious.
BEG_SENT I want to talk very seriously.
BEG_SENT Let me know every thing that I am to know, without delay.
BEG_SENT Will you tell me how long you have loved him?” “It has been coming on so gradually, that I hardly know when it began.
BEG_SENT But I believe I must date it from my first seeing his beautiful grounds at Pemberley.” Another entreaty that she would be serious, however, produced the desired effect; and she soon satisfied Jane by her solemn assurances of attachment.
BEG_SENT When convinced on that article, Miss Bennet had nothing further to wish.
BEG_SENT “Now I am quite happy,” said she, “for you will be as happy as myself.
BEG_SENT I always had a value for him.
BEG_SENT Were it for nothing but his love of you, I must always have esteemed him; but now, as Bingley’s friend and your husband, there can be only Bingley and yourself more dear to me.
BEG_SENT But Lizzy, you have been very sly, very reserved with me.
BEG_SENT How little did you tell me of what passed at Pemberley and Lambton!
BEG_SENT I owe all that I know of it to another, not to you.” Elizabeth told her the motives of her secrecy.
BEG_SENT She had been unwilling to mention Bingley; and the unsettled state of her own feelings had made her equally avoid the name of his friend.
BEG_SENT But now she would no longer conceal from her his share in Lydia’s marriage.
BEG_SENT All was acknowledged, and half the night spent in conversation.
BEG_SENT “Good gracious!” cried Mrs. Bennet, as she stood at a window the next morning, “if that disagreeable Mr. Darcy is not coming here again with our dear Bingley!
BEG_SENT What can he mean by being so tiresome as to be always coming here?
BEG_SENT I had no notion but he would go a-shooting, or something or other, and not disturb us with his company.
BEG_SENT What shall we do with him?
BEG_SENT Lizzy, you must walk out with him again, that he may not be in Bingley’s way.” Elizabeth could hardly help laughing at so convenient a proposal; yet was really vexed that her mother should be always giving him such an epithet.
BEG_SENT As soon as they entered, Bingley looked at her so expressively, and shook hands with such warmth, as left no doubt of his good information; and he soon afterwards said aloud, “Mrs.
BEG_SENT Bennet, have you no more lanes hereabouts in which Lizzy may lose her way again to-day?” “I advise Mr. Darcy, and Lizzy, and Kitty,” said Mrs. Bennet, “to walk to Oakham Mount this morning.
BEG_SENT It is a nice long walk, and Mr. Darcy has never seen the view.” “It may do very well for the others,” replied Mr. Bingley; “but I am sure it will be too much for Kitty.
BEG_SENT Won’t it, Kitty?” Kitty owned that she had rather stay at home.
BEG_SENT Darcy professed a great curiosity to see the view from the Mount, and Elizabeth silently consented.
BEG_SENT As she went up stairs to get ready, Mrs. Bennet followed her, saying: “I am quite sorry, Lizzy, that you should be forced to have that disagreeable man all to yourself.
BEG_SENT But I hope you will not mind it: it is all for Jane’s sake, you know; and there is no occasion for talking to him, except just now and then.
BEG_SENT So, do not put yourself to inconvenience.” During their walk, it was resolved that Mr. Bennet’s consent should be asked in the course of the evening.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth reserved to herself the application for her mother’s.
BEG_SENT She could not determine how her mother would take it; sometimes doubting whether all his wealth and grandeur would be enough to overcome her abhorrence of the man.
BEG_SENT But whether she were violently set against the match, or violently delighted with it, it was certain that her manner would be equally ill adapted to do credit to her sense; and she could no more bear that Mr. Darcy should hear the first raptures of her joy, than the first vehemence of her disapprobation.
BEG_SENT In the evening, soon after Mr. Bennet withdrew to the library, she saw Mr. Darcy rise also and follow him, and her agitation on seeing it was extreme.
BEG_SENT She did not fear her father’s opposition, but he was going to be made unhappy; and that it should be through her means—that _she_, his favourite child, should be distressing him by her choice, should be filling him with fears and regrets in disposing of her—was a wretched reflection, and she sat in misery till Mr. Darcy appeared again, when, looking at him, she was a little relieved by his smile.
BEG_SENT In a few minutes he approached the table where she was sitting with Kitty; and, while pretending to admire her work said in a whisper, “Go to your father, he wants you in the library.” She was gone directly.
BEG_SENT Her father was walking about the room, looking grave and anxious.
BEG_SENT “Lizzy,” said he, “what are you doing?
BEG_SENT Are you out of your senses, to be accepting this man?
BEG_SENT Have not you always hated him?” How earnestly did she then wish that her former opinions had been more reasonable, her expressions more moderate!
BEG_SENT It would have spared her from explanations and professions which it was exceedingly awkward to give; but they were now necessary, and she assured him, with some confusion, of her attachment to Mr. Darcy.
BEG_SENT “Or, in other words, you are determined to have him.
BEG_SENT He is rich, to be sure, and you may have more fine clothes and fine carriages than Jane.
BEG_SENT But will they make you happy?” “Have you any other objection,” said Elizabeth, “than your belief of my indifference?” “None at all.
BEG_SENT We all know him to be a proud, unpleasant sort of man; but this would be nothing if you really liked him.” “I do, I do like him,” she replied, with tears in her eyes, “I love him.
BEG_SENT Indeed he has no improper pride.
BEG_SENT He is perfectly amiable.
BEG_SENT You do not know what he really is; then pray do not pain me by speaking of him in such terms.” “Lizzy,” said her father, “I have given him my consent.
BEG_SENT He is the kind of man, indeed, to whom I should never dare refuse anything, which he condescended to ask.
BEG_SENT I now give it to _you_, if you are resolved on having him.
BEG_SENT But let me advise you to think better of it.
BEG_SENT I know your disposition, Lizzy.
BEG_SENT I know that you could be neither happy nor respectable, unless you truly esteemed your husband; unless you looked up to him as a superior.
BEG_SENT Your lively talents would place you in the greatest danger in an unequal marriage.
BEG_SENT You could scarcely escape discredit and misery.
BEG_SENT My child, let me not have the grief of seeing _you_ unable to respect your partner in life.
BEG_SENT You know not what you are about.” Elizabeth, still more affected, was earnest and solemn in her reply; and at length, by repeated assurances that Mr. Darcy was really the object of her choice, by explaining the gradual change which her estimation of him had undergone, relating her absolute certainty that his affection was not the work of a day, but had stood the test of many months’ suspense, and enumerating with energy all his good qualities, she did conquer her father’s incredulity, and reconcile him to the match.
BEG_SENT “Well, my dear,” said he, when she ceased speaking, “I have no more to say.
BEG_SENT If this be the case, he deserves you.
BEG_SENT I could not have parted with you, my Lizzy, to anyone less worthy.” To complete the favourable impression, she then told him what Mr. Darcy had voluntarily done for Lydia.
BEG_SENT He heard her with astonishment.
BEG_SENT “This is an evening of wonders, indeed!
BEG_SENT And so, Darcy did every thing; made up the match, gave the money, paid the fellow’s debts, and got him his commission!
BEG_SENT So much the better.
BEG_SENT It will save me a world of trouble and economy.
BEG_SENT Had it been your uncle’s doing, I must and _would_ have paid him; but these violent young lovers carry every thing their own way.
BEG_SENT I shall offer to pay him to-morrow; he will rant and storm about his love for you, and there will be an end of the matter.” He then recollected her embarrassment a few days before, on his reading Mr. Collins’s letter; and after laughing at her some time, allowed her at last to go—saying, as she quitted the room, “If any young men come for Mary or Kitty, send them in, for I am quite at leisure.” Elizabeth’s mind was now relieved from a very heavy weight; and, after half an hour’s quiet reflection in her own room, she was able to join the others with tolerable composure.
BEG_SENT Every thing was too recent for gaiety, but the evening passed tranquilly away; there was no longer anything material to be dreaded, and the comfort of ease and familiarity would come in time.
BEG_SENT When her mother went up to her dressing-room at night, she followed her, and made the important communication.
BEG_SENT Its effect was most extraordinary; for on first hearing it, Mrs. Bennet sat quite still, and unable to utter a syllable.
BEG_SENT Nor was it under many, many minutes that she could comprehend what she heard; though not in general backward to credit what was for the advantage of her family, or that came in the shape of a lover to any of them.
BEG_SENT She began at length to recover, to fidget about in her chair, get up, sit down again, wonder, and bless herself.
BEG_SENT “Good gracious!
BEG_SENT Lord bless me!
BEG_SENT only think!
BEG_SENT dear me!
BEG_SENT Mr. Darcy!
BEG_SENT Who would have thought it!
BEG_SENT And is it really true?
BEG_SENT Oh!
BEG_SENT my sweetest Lizzy!
BEG_SENT how rich and how great you will be!
BEG_SENT What pin-money, what jewels, what carriages you will have!
BEG_SENT Jane’s is nothing to it—nothing at all.
BEG_SENT I am so pleased—so happy.
BEG_SENT Such a charming man!—so handsome!
BEG_SENT so tall!—Oh, my dear Lizzy!
BEG_SENT pray apologise for my having disliked him so much before.
BEG_SENT I hope he will overlook it.
BEG_SENT Dear, dear Lizzy.
BEG_SENT A house in town!
BEG_SENT Every thing that is charming!
BEG_SENT Three daughters married!
BEG_SENT Ten thousand a year!
BEG_SENT Oh, Lord!
BEG_SENT What will become of me.
BEG_SENT I shall go distracted.” This was enough to prove that her approbation need not be doubted: and Elizabeth, rejoicing that such an effusion was heard only by herself, soon went away.
BEG_SENT But before she had been three minutes in her own room, her mother followed her.
BEG_SENT “My dearest child,” she cried, “I can think of nothing else!
BEG_SENT Ten thousand a year, and very likely more!
BEG_SENT ’Tis as good as a Lord!
BEG_SENT And a special licence.
BEG_SENT You must and shall be married by a special licence.
BEG_SENT But my dearest love, tell me what dish Mr. Darcy is particularly fond of, that I may have it to-morrow.” This was a sad omen of what her mother’s behaviour to the gentleman himself might be; and Elizabeth found that, though in the certain possession of his warmest affection, and secure of her relations’ consent, there was still something to be wished for.
BEG_SENT But the morrow passed off much better than she expected; for Mrs. Bennet luckily stood in such awe of her intended son-in-law that she ventured not to speak to him, unless it was in her power to offer him any attention, or mark her deference for his opinion.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth had the satisfaction of seeing her father taking pains to get acquainted with him; and Mr. Bennet soon assured her that he was rising every hour in his esteem.
BEG_SENT “I admire all my three sons-in-law highly,” said he.
BEG_SENT “Wickham, perhaps, is my favourite; but I think I shall like _your_ husband quite as well as Jane’s.”   Elizabeth’s spirits soon rising to playfulness again, she wanted Mr. Darcy to account for his having ever fallen in love with her.
BEG_SENT “How could you begin?” said she.
BEG_SENT “I can comprehend your going on charmingly, when you had once made a beginning; but what could set you off in the first place?” “I cannot fix on the hour, or the spot, or the look, or the words, which laid the foundation.
BEG_SENT It is too long ago.
BEG_SENT I was in the middle before I knew that I _had_ begun.” “My beauty you had early withstood, and as for my manners—my behaviour to _you_ was at least always bordering on the uncivil, and I never spoke to you without rather wishing to give you pain than not.
BEG_SENT Now be sincere; did you admire me for my impertinence?” “For the liveliness of your mind, I did.” “You may as well call it impertinence at once.
BEG_SENT It was very little less.
BEG_SENT The fact is, that you were sick of civility, of deference, of officious attention.
BEG_SENT You were disgusted with the women who were always speaking, and looking, and thinking for _your_ approbation alone.
BEG_SENT I roused, and interested you, because I was so unlike _them_.
BEG_SENT Had you not been really amiable, you would have hated me for it; but in spite of the pains you took to disguise yourself, your feelings were always noble and just; and in your heart, you thoroughly despised the persons who so assiduously courted you.
BEG_SENT There—I have saved you the trouble of accounting for it; and really, all things considered, I begin to think it perfectly reasonable.
BEG_SENT To be sure, you knew no actual good of me—but nobody thinks of _that_ when they fall in love.” “Was there no good in your affectionate behaviour to Jane while she was ill at Netherfield?” “Dearest Jane!
BEG_SENT who could have done less for her?
BEG_SENT But make a virtue of it by all means.
BEG_SENT My good qualities are under your protection, and you are to exaggerate them as much as possible; and, in return, it belongs to me to find occasions for teasing and quarrelling with you as often as may be; and I shall begin directly by asking you what made you so unwilling to come to the point at last.
BEG_SENT What made you so shy of me, when you first called, and afterwards dined here?
BEG_SENT Why, especially, when you called, did you look as if you did not care about me?” “Because you were grave and silent, and gave me no encouragement.” “But I was embarrassed.” “And so was I.” “You might have talked to me more when you came to dinner.” “A man who had felt less, might.” “How unlucky that you should have a reasonable answer to give, and that I should be so reasonable as to admit it!
BEG_SENT But I wonder how long you _would_ have gone on, if you had been left to yourself.
BEG_SENT I wonder when you _would_ have spoken, if I had not asked you!
BEG_SENT My resolution of thanking you for your kindness to Lydia had certainly great effect.
BEG_SENT _Too much_, I am afraid; for what becomes of the moral, if our comfort springs from a breach of promise?
BEG_SENT for I ought not to have mentioned the subject.
BEG_SENT This will never do.” “You need not distress yourself.
BEG_SENT The moral will be perfectly fair.
BEG_SENT Lady Catherine’s unjustifiable endeavours to separate us were the means of removing all my doubts.
BEG_SENT I am not indebted for my present happiness to your eager desire of expressing your gratitude.
BEG_SENT I was not in a humour to wait for any opening of yours.
BEG_SENT My aunt’s intelligence had given me hope, and I was determined at once to know every thing.” “Lady Catherine has been of infinite use, which ought to make her happy, for she loves to be of use.
BEG_SENT But tell me, what did you come down to Netherfield for?
BEG_SENT Was it merely to ride to Longbourn and be embarrassed?
BEG_SENT or had you intended any more serious consequence?” “My real purpose was to see _you_, and to judge, if I could, whether I might ever hope to make you love me.
BEG_SENT My avowed one, or what I avowed to myself, was to see whether your sister were still partial to Bingley, and if she were, to make the confession to him which I have since made.” “Shall you ever have courage to announce to Lady Catherine what is to befall her?” “I am more likely to want more time than courage, Elizabeth.
BEG_SENT But it ought to be done, and if you will give me a sheet of paper, it shall be done directly.” “And if I had not a letter to write myself, I might sit by you and admire the evenness of your writing, as another young lady once did.
BEG_SENT But I have an aunt, too, who must not be longer neglected.” From an unwillingness to confess how much her intimacy with Mr. Darcy had been over-rated, Elizabeth had never yet answered Mrs. Gardiner’s long letter; but now, having _that_ to communicate which she knew would be most welcome, she was almost ashamed to find that her uncle and aunt had already lost three days of happiness, and immediately wrote as follows: “I would have thanked you before, my dear aunt, as I ought to have done, for your long, kind, satisfactory, detail of particulars; but to say the truth, I was too cross to write.
BEG_SENT You supposed more than really existed.
BEG_SENT But _now_ suppose as much as you choose; give a loose rein to your fancy, indulge your imagination in every possible flight which the subject will afford, and unless you believe me actually married, you cannot greatly err.
BEG_SENT You must write again very soon, and praise him a great deal more than you did in your last.
BEG_SENT I thank you, again and again, for not going to the Lakes.
BEG_SENT How could I be so silly as to wish it!
BEG_SENT Your idea of the ponies is delightful.
BEG_SENT We will go round the Park every day.
BEG_SENT I am the happiest creature in the world.
BEG_SENT Perhaps other people have said so before, but not one with such justice.
BEG_SENT I am happier even than Jane; she only smiles, I laugh.
BEG_SENT Mr. Darcy sends you all the love in the world that he can spare from me.
BEG_SENT You are all to come to Pemberley at Christmas.
BEG_SENT Yours, etc.” Mr. Darcy’s letter to Lady Catherine was in a different style; and still different from either was what Mr. Bennet sent to Mr. Collins, in reply to his last.
BEG_SENT “Dear Sir, “I must trouble you once more for congratulations.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth will soon be the wife of Mr. Darcy.
BEG_SENT Console Lady Catherine as well as you can.
BEG_SENT But, if I were you, I would stand by the nephew.
BEG_SENT He has more to give.
BEG_SENT “Yours sincerely, etc.” Miss Bingley’s congratulations to her brother, on his approaching marriage, were all that was affectionate and insincere.
BEG_SENT She wrote even to Jane on the occasion, to express her delight, and repeat all her former professions of regard.
BEG_SENT Jane was not deceived, but she was affected; and though feeling no reliance on her, could not help writing her a much kinder answer than she knew was deserved.
BEG_SENT The joy which Miss Darcy expressed on receiving similar information, was as sincere as her brother’s in sending it.
BEG_SENT Four sides of paper were insufficient to contain all her delight, and all her earnest desire of being loved by her sister.
BEG_SENT Before any answer could arrive from Mr. Collins, or any congratulations to Elizabeth from his wife, the Longbourn family heard that the Collinses were come themselves to Lucas Lodge.
BEG_SENT The reason of this sudden removal was soon evident.
BEG_SENT Lady Catherine had been rendered so exceedingly angry by the contents of her nephew’s letter, that Charlotte, really rejoicing in the match, was anxious to get away till the storm was blown over.
BEG_SENT At such a moment, the arrival of her friend was a sincere pleasure to Elizabeth, though in the course of their meetings she must sometimes think the pleasure dearly bought, when she saw Mr. Darcy exposed to all the parading and obsequious civility of her husband.
BEG_SENT He bore it, however, with admirable calmness.
BEG_SENT He could even listen to Sir William Lucas, when he complimented him on carrying away the brightest jewel of the country, and expressed his hopes of their all meeting frequently at St. James’s, with very decent composure.
BEG_SENT If he did shrug his shoulders, it was not till Sir William was out of sight.
BEG_SENT Mrs. Phillips’s vulgarity was another, and perhaps a greater, tax on his forbearance; and though Mrs. Phillips, as well as her sister, stood in too much awe of him to speak with the familiarity which Bingley’s good humour encouraged, yet, whenever she _did_ speak, she must be vulgar.
BEG_SENT Nor was her respect for him, though it made her more quiet, at all likely to make her more elegant.
BEG_SENT Elizabeth did all she could to shield him from the frequent notice of either, and was ever anxious to keep him to herself, and to those of her family with whom he might converse without mortification; and though the uncomfortable feelings arising from all this took from the season of courtship much of its pleasure, it added to the hope of the future; and she looked forward with delight to the time when they should be removed from society so little pleasing to either, to all the comfort and elegance of their family party at Pemberley.
BEG_SENT   Happy for all her maternal feelings was the day on which Mrs. Bennet got rid of her two most deserving daughters.
BEG_SENT With what delighted pride she afterwards visited Mrs. Bingley, and talked of Mrs. Darcy, may be guessed.
BEG_SENT I wish I could say, for the sake of her family, that the accomplishment of her earnest desire in the establishment of so many of her children produced so happy an effect as to make her a sensible, amiable, well-informed woman for the rest of her life; though perhaps it was lucky for her husband, who might not have relished domestic felicity in so unusual a form, that she still was occasionally nervous and invariably silly.
BEG_SENT Mr. Bennet missed his second daughter exceedingly; his affection for her drew him oftener from home than anything else could do.
BEG_SENT He delighted in going to Pemberley, especially when he was least expected.
BEG_SENT Mr. Bingley and Jane remained at Netherfield only a twelvemonth.
BEG_SENT So near a vicinity to her mother and Meryton relations was not desirable even to _his_ easy temper, or _her_ affectionate heart.
BEG_SENT The darling wish of his sisters was then gratified; he bought an estate in a neighbouring county to Derbyshire, and Jane and Elizabeth, in addition to every other source of happiness, were within thirty miles of each other.
BEG_SENT Kitty, to her very material advantage, spent the chief of her time with her two elder sisters.
BEG_SENT In society so superior to what she had generally known, her improvement was great.
BEG_SENT She was not of so ungovernable a temper as Lydia; and, removed from the influence of Lydia’s example, she became, by proper attention and management, less irritable, less ignorant, and less insipid.
BEG_SENT From the further disadvantage of Lydia’s society she was of course carefully kept, and though Mrs. Wickham frequently invited her to come and stay with her, with the promise of balls and young men, her father would never consent to her going.
BEG_SENT Mary was the only daughter who remained at home; and she was necessarily drawn from the pursuit of accomplishments by Mrs. Bennet’s being quite unable to sit alone.
BEG_SENT Mary was obliged to mix more with the world, but she could still moralize over every morning visit; and as she was no longer mortified by comparisons between her sisters’ beauty and her own, it was suspected by her father that she submitted to the change without much reluctance.
BEG_SENT As for Wickham and Lydia, their characters suffered no revolution from the marriage of her sisters.
BEG_SENT He bore with philosophy the conviction that Elizabeth must now become acquainted with whatever of his ingratitude and falsehood had before been unknown to her; and in spite of every thing, was not wholly without hope that Darcy might yet be prevailed on to make his fortune.
BEG_SENT The congratulatory letter which Elizabeth received from Lydia on her marriage, explained to her that, by his wife at least, if not by himself, such a hope was cherished.
BEG_SENT The letter was to this effect: “My dear Lizzy, “I wish you joy.
BEG_SENT If you love Mr. Darcy half as well as I do my dear Wickham, you must be very happy.
BEG_SENT It is a great comfort to have you so rich, and when you have nothing else to do, I hope you will think of us.
BEG_SENT I am sure Wickham would like a place at court very much, and I do not think we shall have quite money enough to live upon without some help.
BEG_SENT Any place would do, of about three or four hundred a year; but however, do not speak to Mr. Darcy about it, if you had rather not.
BEG_SENT “Yours, etc.” As it happened that Elizabeth had much rather not, she endeavoured in her answer to put an end to every entreaty and expectation of the kind.
BEG_SENT Such relief, however, as it was in her power to afford, by the practice of what might be called economy in her own private expences, she frequently sent them.
BEG_SENT It had always been evident to her that such an income as theirs, under the direction of two persons so extravagant in their wants, and heedless of the future, must be very insufficient to their support; and whenever they changed their quarters, either Jane or herself were sure of being applied to for some little assistance towards discharging their bills.
BEG_SENT Their manner of living, even when the restoration of peace dismissed them to a home, was unsettled in the extreme.
BEG_SENT They were always moving from place to place in quest of a cheap situation, and always spending more than they ought.
BEG_SENT His affection for her soon sunk into indifference; hers lasted a little longer; and in spite of her youth and her manners, she retained all the claims to reputation which her marriage had given her.
BEG_SENT Though Darcy could never receive _him_ at Pemberley, yet, for Elizabeth’s sake, he assisted him further in his profession.
BEG_SENT Lydia was occasionally a visitor there, when her husband was gone to enjoy himself in London or Bath; and with the Bingleys they both of them frequently staid so long, that even Bingley’s good humour was overcome, and he proceeded so far as to _talk_ of giving them a hint to be gone.
BEG_SENT Miss Bingley was very deeply mortified by Darcy’s marriage; but as she thought it advisable to retain the right of visiting at Pemberley, she dropt all her resentment; was fonder than ever of Georgiana, almost as attentive to Darcy as heretofore, and paid off every arrear of civility to Elizabeth.
BEG_SENT Pemberley was now Georgiana’s home; and the attachment of the sisters was exactly what Darcy had hoped to see.
BEG_SENT They were able to love each other even as well as they intended.
BEG_SENT Georgiana had the highest opinion in the world of Elizabeth; though at first she often listened with an astonishment bordering on alarm at her lively, sportive, manner of talking to her brother.
BEG_SENT He, who had always inspired in herself a respect which almost overcame her affection, she now saw the object of open pleasantry.
BEG_SENT Her mind received knowledge which had never before fallen in her way.
BEG_SENT By Elizabeth’s instructions, she began to comprehend that a woman may take liberties with her husband which a brother will not always allow in a sister more than ten years younger than himself.
BEG_SENT Lady Catherine was extremely indignant on the marriage of her nephew; and as she gave way to all the genuine frankness of her character in her reply to the letter which announced its arrangement, she sent him language so very abusive, especially of Elizabeth, that for some time all intercourse was at an end.
BEG_SENT But at length, by Elizabeth’s persuasion, he was prevailed on to overlook the offence, and seek a reconciliation; and, after a little further resistance on the part of his aunt, her resentment gave way, either to her affection for him, or her curiosity to see how his wife conducted herself; and she condescended to wait on them at Pemberley, in spite of that pollution which its woods had received, not merely from the presence of such a mistress, but the visits of her uncle and aunt from the city.
BEG_SENT With the Gardiners, they were always on the most intimate terms.
BEG_SENT Darcy, as well as Elizabeth, really loved them; and they were both ever sensible of the warmest gratitude towards the persons who, by bringing her into Derbyshire, had been the means of uniting them.
